Preface

Student Notes:

A Message to the Dedicated Mains 2025 Aspirant
Dear Aspirant,
Congratulations on conquering the first stage of the Civil Services Examination. The journey to
Mains tests not just knowledge, but strategy, endurance, and the ability to articulate nuanced
perspectives under pressure.

The Unique Challenge of Indian Society
The Indian Society section of GS Paper 1 presents a unique challenge. Unlike more static
subjects, it lacks a single, reliable textbook. This is compounded by the dynamism of the subject
itself; driven by the rapid impacts of technology and globalization, society is in constant flux.
Reflecting this, UPSC questions have become progressively more analytical and challenging,
demanding nuanced insights that go far beyond rote facts—a gap that most market materials
fail to fill.

Our Philosophy: Precision and Relevance through Analysis
It is with this understanding that we have crafted this Indian Society Value Added Material
(VAM). Our philosophy is grounded in precision and relevance. We have meticulously designed
this material to bridge the gap between conventional resources and the UPSC's evolving
expectations.
To ground your preparation in the reality of the exam, key chapters are prefaced with a list of
relevant Previous Year Questions (PYQs). This analysis will help you identify high-priority themes
and understand the depth required for each topic, ensuring your efforts are sharply focused.

How This VAM Empowers Your Preparation
Our primary goal is to equip you with the content and confidence to write answers that stand
out. This VAM is structured to achieve several key objectives:
•
•
•
•

Go Beyond Factual Recitation: This VAM provides analytical frameworks and critical
perspectives to help you understand the 'why' and 'how' behind social phenomena, not just
the 'what'.
Arm Yourself with Contemporary Content: The document is enriched with updated data,
key facts, and essential sociological terminologies that are crucial for writing high-scoring
answers.
Master Substantiation with Case Studies: A strong answer is a well-substantiated one. This
material is packed with an ample number of relevant case studies to add weight, credibility,
and uniqueness to your arguments.
Stay Ahead with a High-Impact Add-on Chapter: We have included a dedicated chapter on
the "Impact of Social Media and AI," a highly relevant theme. The insights here will be
invaluable not just for GS-1, but can also be effectively utilized in the Essay and GS-4 Ethics
papers.

Our Commitment to Your Success
This document is a culmination of dedicated effort to simplify your preparation. Our goal is to
empower you to not just understand Indian society but to analyze it critically. Trust this resource,
integrate its insights, and walk into the examination hall with the confidence to tackle the most
demanding questions.
We wish you the very best.

Sudden Spike

3. GS-1 Society: Advice from Vision IAS Experts
Moving beyond the objective data of the marks-distribution table, it is crucial to decode the DNA
of the GS-1 Society paper. This expert analysis, curated by Vision IAS experts, translates the raw
data into an actionable strategy, helping you think like the examiner and understand the "why"
behind the questions. The goal is to help you prepare this dynamic paper with confidence and
clarity.
•

•

•

Master the Four Pillars: Focus on the High-Yield Zones
o Your preparation must be anchored in the four themes that consistently form the paper's
core. The examiners repeatedly probe the foundations of our social fabric, asking deep,
structural questions.
o You must master the concepts surrounding India's salient features (including caste,
family, and diversity), the evolving role and challenges of women, the multifaceted
impact of globalization, and the critical trifecta of communalism, secularism, and
regionalism.
o A significant portion of your time should be dedicated here, as a deep dive into a
question like, "How does Indian society maintain continuity in traditional social values?
Enumerate the changes taking place in it?" [2021, 15 Marks], requires a thorough
command of these foundational topics.
Go Beyond Description: The Demand for Critical and Analytical Depth
o The Society paper is not a test of memory; it is a test of your analytical skill. You are rarely
asked to simply describe a social phenomenon. Instead, the questions push you to
evaluate its real-world impact and take a well-argued stand. For example, you are not
just asked about urbanization, but are challenged to critically analyse if it leads to
negative outcomes, as seen in the 2023 question: "Does urbanization lead to more
segregation and/or marginalization of the poor in Indian metropolises?".
o The key is to move beyond description and substantiate your arguments, exploring the
underlying causes and consequences of every issue.
Connect the Dots: Think Holistically, Not in Silos
o Social forces do not operate in isolation, and neither should your preparation. The most
insightful questions reflect this interconnectedness, weaving together multiple syllabus
topics.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

8

www.visionias.in

# 8468022022

©Vision IAS

o

•

A top-tier answer demonstrates how globalization impacts gender roles, which in turn
affects family structures and urban migration.
o Notice how the 2024 question, "Globalisation has increased urban migration by skilled,
young, unmarried women... How has this trend impacted upon their personal freedom
and relationship with family?", masterfully links globalization, women's issues,
urbanization, and the family unit in a single, compelling query. To score well, you must
connect these dots seamlessly in your answers.
Ground Your Answers in the Present: Feel the Contemporary Pulse
o This paper has its finger firmly on the pulse of modern India. Theoretical knowledge must
be illustrated with what is happening now. The COVID-19 pandemic, for instance, directly
prompted the 2022 question, "Explore and evaluate the impact of ‘Work From Home’ on
family relationships." Similarly, the rise of new technologies led to questions on the gig
economy's effect on women.
o This proves that your ability to link timeless social concepts with contemporary events
is non-negotiable. You must ground every topic in your notes with fresh, relevant
examples from the world around you.

CHAPTER 1: SALIENT FEATURES OF INDIAN SOCIETY
Key Data & Facts at a Glance
•

•

•

Demographic & Linguistic Diversity:
o India has more than 19,500 languages or dialects spoken as mother tongues(Census
2011). However, only 121 are classified as languages and 22 in the 8th Schedule.
o 22 languages are listed in the 8th Schedule of the Constitution, with about 97% of the
population having one of these as their mother tongue.
o Religious Composition (Census 2011): Hindus (79.8%), Muslims (14.2%), Christians
(2.3%), Sikhs (1.7%), Buddhists (0.7%), Jains (0.4%).
Social Structure: Caste & Tribe:
o Scheduled Castes (SCs) comprise 16.6% and Scheduled Tribes (STs) comprise 8.6% of
the population (Census 2011).
o Caste-Based Violence: 57,582 cases of caste-based crimes were reported in 2022
(NCRB).
o Caste and Wealth Inequality: India's top 10% own 77% of the national wealth. An
Oxfam report highlights deep correlation with caste, with upper castes holding a
disproportionate share.
o Caste in Politics: 80% of political parties field candidates based on caste calculus
(CSDS, 2019).
o Sex Ratio Disparity: Sex ratio among Tribes is more favourable to women than among
Scheduled Castes, often attributed to lower prevalence of dowry and different social
structures in many tribal communities.
Social Institutions: Family & Marriage:
o Joint vs. Nuclear Households: Only about 20% of households are traditional joint or
extended families (Census 2011). Nuclear families now make up about 50% of
households (2022 data).
o Inter-Caste Marriages: Remain low at 11.4% (NFHS-5, 2021), though rates are higher
in urban areas.
o Love marriages: Love marriages increased from 3% (1970) to 12% (2020), driven by
education and urbanization (Allendorf & Pandian, 2016).
o Self-arranged marriages: 72% of urban youth prefer "self-arranged" marriages but
seek family approval (ISS, 2021).
o Caste endogamy: About 60% of Shaadi.com users still prioritize caste (Thomas & K.,
2019).
o Inter-Religious Marriages: Even rarer, at around 2.1%. Recent controversies around
anti-conversion laws in several states reflect the societal and political resistance to
such unions.
o Single-Parent Households: An estimated 4.5% of all Indian households are run by
single mothers (UN Report, 2019).

Glossary of Essential Concepts
•

•
•

•
•

•
•
•

•
•

•

•
•
•

Student Notes:

Diversity vs. Pluralism: Diversity refers to the simple existence of variety (linguistic,
religious, etc.) within a society. Pluralism, a more profound concept, implies a positive
valuation and active engagement with this diversity, where different groups maintain
their unique identities while participating in a common national life.
Unity in Diversity: The core philosophy of Indian nationalism, suggesting that beneath
the surface of immense diversity lies a fundamental, civilizational unity bound by a shared
history, cultural syncretism, and constitutional values.
Secularism (Indian Context): Unlike the Western model of a strict "wall of separation"
between state and religion, Indian secularism is based on the principle of "principled
distance," where the state treats all religions with equal respect and can intervene to
ensure social justice and reform.
Caste (Varna vs. Jati): A foundational concept. Varna is the four-fold textual/ideological
model (Brahmin, Kshatriya, Vaishya, Shudra). Jati is the complex system of thousands of
endogamous local communities that represents the lived, operational reality of caste.
Sanskritization & De-Sanskritization (M.N. Srinivas): Sanskritization is the process
where lower or middle castes adopt the rituals and practices of higher castes to improve
their social status. De-Sanskritization refers to the opposite process, where dominant
castes may adopt secular or non-traditional practices.
Dominant Caste (M.N. Srinivas): A key concept for understanding rural power dynamics,
referring to a caste that is numerically strong in a local area and wields significant
economic and political power.
Communalism: An ideology that promotes allegiance to a religious community over the
larger society or nation, often fostering hostility towards other religious groups.
Regionalism: An expression of a shared identity based on a specific geographical region,
often rooted in common language, culture, and history. It can range from demands for
statehood and autonomy (sub-nationalism) to negative, chauvinistic assertions against
outsiders (parochialism).
Globalization: The process of intensifying social, economic, cultural, and political linkages
across the globe.
Cultural Homogenization vs. Glocalization vs. Cultural Hybridity: Theoretical outcomes
of globalization's cultural impact. Homogenization is the fear of a single, dominant global
culture (e.g., Americanization). Glocalization is the process where global products are
adapted to local cultures (e.g., McAloo Tikki). Cultural Hybridity refers to the creation of
new, mixed cultural forms.
Sacrament vs. Contract (in Marriage): A key axis of social change. The sacramental view
treats marriage as a sacred, indissoluble religious union. The contractual view sees it as a
partnership based on companionship, individual choice, and mutual consent, which can
be dissolved.
Obscurantism: The practice of deliberately resisting reason, inquiry, and enlightenment,
often by appealing to tradition or dogma to suppress critical thought.
Social Capital: The networks of relationships among people who live and work in a
particular society, enabling that society to function effectively. The decline of joint
families or community interactions can be analyzed as an erosion of social capital.
Patriarchy: A social system where men hold primary power and authority over women,
controlling resources, mobility, and decision-making. While a core concept for the
"Women" topic, it is also a salient feature of India's overall social structure.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

www.visionias.in

©Vision IAS

Introduction

Student Notes:

Indian society, often described as a 'Civilizational State', is marked by unparalleled historical
depth and cultural complexity. Its most defining characteristic is the dynamic interplay between
Continuity and Change. While ancient social institutions and values persist, they are constantly
being reshaped by modern forces, creating a social fabric that is both resilient and adaptive.

India as a Civilizational State
India is often considered a civilizational state due to its continuous cultural and historical
identity that spans over 5,000 years. Unlike a typical nation-state, India’s identity is shaped by
its diverse yet interconnected traditions, including Hinduism, Buddhism, Jainism, and various
regional cultures.
This shared cultural heritage, coupled with its ancient social and philosophical practices,
defines India’s unique position in the world. India’s unity in diversity is a testament to its deeprooted civilizational continuity. Other examples of civilizational states include China, Russia,
Egypt etc.
The uniqueness of Indian society lies in its remarkable ability to assimilate diverse external
influences—from ancient invaders to modern globalization—without losing its fundamental
cultural ethos. This process of absorption and synthesis makes India a fascinating and complex
subject of study.

1. The Foundations of Indian Society: Traditional
Structures and Values
To understand contemporary India, we must first appreciate the foundational pillars of its
traditional social structure. These enduring features have shaped Indian society for centuries and
continue to influence its present.

1.1. The Principle of Diversity
India's most celebrated salient feature is its immense diversity, which is not just a collection of
differences but a lived reality shaping its identity.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

17

# 8468022022

©Vision IAS

•

•

•

•

Linguistic Diversity: India is a mosaic of
languages. The 1961 Census listed 1,652
mother tongues, while later surveys have
identified over 19,500 languages or
dialects. As per the Census 2011, there are
121 languages spoken by 10,000 or more
people, and 22 of these are officially
recognized in the Eighth Schedule of the
Constitution. This deep-rooted linguistic
identity was the primary basis for the
reorganization
of
states
after
Independence.
Religious Diversity: India is the birthplace of four
major world religions—Hinduism, Buddhism,
Jainism, and Sikhism—and has been a welcoming
home to others like Islam, Christianity, Judaism, and
Zoroastrianism for centuries. This has led to not just
coexistence but often a unique syncretism, where
beliefs and practices have intermingled.
Ethnic and Racial Diversity: The Indian population is a
blend of major racial groups—Caucasoid, Mongoloid,
and Negroid—along with other groups like the
Australoid, contributing to a rich tapestry of physical
features and ethnic identities.
Manifestations in Tangible and Intangible Culture:
This diversity is vividly expressed in everyday life:
○ Festivals: From harvest festivals like Onam in
Kerala and Baisakhi in Punjab to religious
celebrations like Eid and Durga Puja,
festivals vary widely across regions and

1.2. The Structure of Hierarchy: The Caste System (Jati and Varna)
The caste system has been the most significant and defining feature of India's traditional social
hierarchy. It is crucial to distinguish between its two forms:
AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

18

www.visionias.in

# 8468022022

©Vision IAS

Student Notes:

•

•

Student Notes:

Varna: This is the four-fold textual or
ideological model found in ancient texts,
dividing society into four hierarchical
categories: Brahmin, Kshatriya, Vaishya,
and Shudra.
Jati: It is a complex system of thousands
of local, endogamous communities, each
with a traditional occupation, social
status, and rules of interaction. As per
scholars, it is the Jati, not the Varna, that
has been the "real and effective unit" of
the caste system.

The traditional Jati system was characterized
by:
•
•
•

•

Hereditary Membership: Caste status
was determined by birth and was
immutable.
Endogamy: Marriage was strictly
restricted to within one's own Jati.
Occupational Specialization: Each Jati
was associated with a traditional
occupation, often linked through the
Jajmani system.
Hierarchy and Purity-Pollution: Jatis were
arranged in a rigid hierarchy based on
concepts of ritual purity and pollution,
which governed social interactions and was
the basis for the practice of untouchability.

1.3. The Structure of Community:
Family, Kinship, and Village
•

•

The
Traditional
Joint
Family:
The ideal and prevalent form in traditional
India was the joint family. This was a multigenerational patriarchal unit where members shared a
common residence, kitchen, and property. As a social
institution, it performed several key functions as
mentioned in the diagram.
Kinship System: Beyond the family, life was deeply
embedded in extensive kinship networks. These ties,
both of blood (consanguineous) and marriage
(affinal), defined an individual's rights, duties, and
social circle, forming the bedrock of social
organization.

"Unity in Diversity": Philosophy and Practice
The idea of "Unity in Diversity" is not just a modern slogan but a historical reality rooted in
the Indian ethos. It reflects a spirit of assimilation and coexistence that has allowed India to
sustain its culture through millennia.
Philosophical Roots & Syncretism in Practice:
•
•

•

Ancient Philosophy: The concept of 'Vasudhaiva Kutumbakam' (the world is one family)
reflects a historical spirit of tolerance and acceptance.
Religious Syncretism: The Bhakti and Sufi movements of the medieval period created
powerful spiritual traditions that transcended religious divides. Shared sacred sites, like
the Ajmer Sharif Dargah in Rajasthan, are venerated by all, showcasing a living tradition
of syncretism.
Cultural Hybridity: This unity is also visible in Indo-Islamic architecture, the development
of the Urdu language (a blend of Persian and local dialects), and various art forms.

Contemporary Manifestations:
This ethos continues today. Large metropolitan cities like Delhi, Mumbai, or Bengaluru are
often described as "cultural pockets of small India". Here, people from every region of the
country live and work together, participating in each other's festivals (like Durga Puja, Ganesh
Chaturthi, Eid), creating a new, cosmopolitan form of unity amidst diversity.

2. The Great Churn: Forces of Change in Modern India
The traditional social structures of India,
while resilient, have been subjected to
immense pressure from powerful forces of
change, particularly since the mid-20th
century.

2.1.
Constitutionalism,
Democracy, and Law
The Constitution of India seeks to replace
the traditional principles of hierarchy and
inequality with the modern values of liberty,
equality, and fraternity.
•

•

•

Universal Adult Franchise: The
introduction of one person, one vote,
provided a powerful tool of political
empowerment for marginalized groups,
which enabled them to challenge the
authority of traditionally dominant
castes.
Fundamental Rights & Legal Reforms: It
provided a legal framework to fight age-old discrimination. Key articles like Art. 15
(prohibition of discrimination), Art. 17 (abolition of untouchability), and Art. 21 (right to life
and dignity) became the basis for social justice movements and legal interventions.
Affirmative Action (Reservations): The policy of reservations for SCs, STs, and OBCs in
education and government jobs was a direct structural intervention designed to rectify
historical injustices and promote upward social mobility.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

The Constitution as a Social Revolution
The Indian Constitution is often viewed as a political or legal document, but socially, it can be
understood as a manifesto for social revolution. It launched a direct assault on the core
principles that underpinned traditional Indian society for centuries.
Principle of Traditional Constitutional Counter-Principle
Society

Key Instrument/Article

Hierarchy (Based on caste, Equality (All citizens are equal before the Articles 14, 15, 17
gender, age)
law)
Holism (Community over Individualism (The individual is the basic Articles 19, 21
the individual)
unit, with fundamental rights)
Continuity (Based on Change & Justice (State empowered to Directive Principles of State
heredity and tradition)
make laws for social reform and justice)
Policy
The ongoing tensions and changes in Indian society can be seen as the unfolding of this
revolutionary agenda.

2.2. Planned Development and Industrialization
The post-independence state adopted a model of planned development through Five-Year
Plans, with a strong emphasis on industrialization. This had far-reaching social consequences:
•
•

New Economic Opportunities: It created new, non-caste-based jobs in factories, public
sector enterprises, and new cities, offering avenues for occupational mobility outside the
traditional jajmani system.
New Class Formations: It led to the emergence of new social classes, such as a modern
industrial working class and a new professional/managerial middle class, which often
coexisted with older caste identities.

Case Study: The Green Revolution's Social Impact
The Green Revolution in the 1960s, a state-led development initiative, transformed Indian
agriculture. While it successfully boosted food production, its social impact was complex:
•
•

Economic Disparity: It primarily benefited large and medium farmers in specific regions
(Punjab, Haryana, Western UP) who could afford the new technology (HYV seeds,
fertilizers, machinery), significantly increasing class inequality within villages.
Regional Imbalance: It exacerbated developmental differences between irrigated
western/northern states and the rain-fed regions of eastern and central India.

This shows how even well-intentioned development projects can have unintended social
consequences, creating new forms of inequality.

2.3. Urbanization
The steady shift of population from rural villages to urban centers has been a powerful solvent
of traditional social norms.
•

Anonymity and Weaker Caste Norms: The anonymity of city life makes the enforcement of
rigid caste rules regarding interaction, occupation, and purity-pollution difficult. While caste
identity does not disappear, its public manifestation is often weakened.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

21

www.visionias.in

# 8468022022

©Vision IAS

•

New Forms of Association: Urban life fosters new, non-caste-based social relationships
based on occupation, neighborhood, or shared interests, creating a more complex social
environment.

2.4. Modern, Secular Education
The spread of a modern, secular education system, theoretically open to all, has been a critical
agent of change.
•
•

Social Mobility: For generations of Dalits, Adivasis, and women, education has been the most
important pathway to upward social mobility, enabling entry into professions and the middle
class.
Spread of Modern Values: Modern education has been instrumental in disseminating
modern, liberal values such as rationalism, individualism, democracy, and equality, which
directly challenge traditional hierarchical values.

2.5. Technology and Mass Media
The penetration of mass media—from print and radio in the early years to television and now
the internet and social media—has fundamentally altered Indian society.
•
•
•

National Consciousness: It has helped create a shared "national imagination" by connecting
disparate parts of the country and broadcasting common cultural content.
Exposure to New Lifestyles: Media has exposed even remote villages to urban and global
lifestyles, influencing aspirations, consumption patterns, and social norms.
New Platforms for Mobilization: Technology has provided new tools for social and political
mobilization, allowing previously voiceless groups to organize and articulate their demands.

2.6. Globalization
While its cultural impact will be discussed later, globalization since the 1990s has acted as a
powerful accelerator for all the above forces. It has intensified economic integration, sped up
the flow of information and cultural products, and created new forms of work, further churning
India's traditional social structures.

3. Impact and Transformation - Caste, Tribe, and Identity
Politics
The forces of change discussed in the previous module have created a dynamic churn within
India's traditional social structures. This has not led to a simple erosion of old identities but to
their complex transformation, persistence, and re-articulation in the modern context.

3.1. Caste in the 21st Century: Fluidity, Persistence, and New Forms
The most common debate regarding the caste system is whether it is weakening or
strengthening. The sociological consensus is that it is doing both simultaneously—weakening in
some aspects while re-emerging in new, powerful forms.
1. The Persistence of Caste: While its ritualistic aspects (purity-pollution) have declined in
public life, caste's influence persists strongly in key areas:
A. Caste in the Social Sphere
• Caste Endogamy: Marriage within one's caste remains a dominant norm in India.
○ Only 11.4% of marriages are inter-caste (NFHS-5, 2021), with the figure dropping
to 5% in rural Bihar/UP.
○ Online, a 2019 study published in the EPW found that over 60% of profiles
mention caste preferencesprofiles on Shaadi.com specify caste.
○ Offline, honour killings for mixed marriages remain a grim reality.
• Segregation and Purity-Pollution:
AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

22

www.visionias.in

# 8468022022

©Vision IAS

Student Notes:

○

47% of rural respondents admit to using separate utensils for Dalits (Social
Attitudes Survey, 2023).
○ 33% of Dalit employees hide their caste at work to avoid discrimination (Equality
Labs Report, 2023).
• Social Networks:
○ Caste remains a primary basis for forming networks to gain access to jobs,
promotions, and business opportunities.
B. Caste in Politics
Caste is a central axis of Indian politics, used for both mobilization and competition.
• Electoral Politics: Parties often select candidates and appeal to voters based on
caste interests, which strengthens caste identity as groups compete for resources
and representation.
• Caste-based Mobilization:
○ Many castes (e.g., Yadavs in UP/Bihar, Mahars in Maharashtra) have used
politics to secure government benefits and representation.
○ Caste alliances are formed to achieve political power (e.g., Patidar-Kshatriya
alliance in Gujarat, Mandal-Kamandal politics).
C. Caste and the Economy
Caste continues to significantly influence economic outcomes and opportunities.
• Wealth and Income Inequality: There is a stark correlation between caste and
wealth.
○ 22.3% of higher caste Hindus own 41% of the nation's wealth, while 7.8% of STs
own only 3.7% (SPPU-JNU Study, 2019).
○ Poverty is disproportionately high among lower castes: the headcount ratio is
50.6% for STs and 33.3% for SCs, compared to 15.6% for 'Others' (2015-16 data).
• Caste in Urban and Formal Sectors:
○ Dalits constitute 20.3% of the slum population in urban India, significantly
higher than their overall urban share of 12.6%.
○ 94% of manual scavengers are SC/ST (Safai Karmachari Andolan report).
D. Caste-Based Violence
The assertion of basic rights by Dalits often triggers violent backlash.
• The increasing trend of violence is linked to instances of inter-caste marriage and
Dalits demanding rights to land, justice, and education.
• The Una attack in Gujarat, where Dalits were assaulted for demanding land
ownership, is a prominent example.
• NCRB 2022 data shows over 67,000 cases were registered under the SC/ST
(Prevention of Atrocities) Act.
2. The Fluidity and New Forms of Caste: Caste has proven to be remarkably adaptive to
democratic politics and a market economy.
• From Ritual Hierarchy to Political Mobilization: Caste has transformed from a vertical
ritual hierarchy into a tool for political mobilization in the form of horizontal identity
blocs. It has become the basis for forming pressure groups and vote banks to demand
state benefits and political power.
• New Associational Forms: The traditional Jati panchayat has given way to modern,
formal caste associations (e.g., Kshatriya Mahasabha, Nadar Sangam) that run
educational institutions, lobby the government, and work for the welfare of their
members. This reflects a "horizontal stretch" of caste, where modern communications
unite caste members across regions.
• Caste as Identity: For many, especially among the youth and diaspora, caste is
increasingly being articulated not just as a structure of discrimination but also as a source
of cultural identity, history, and pride.

'Caste in Everyday Life': Beyond Structure, Towards Affect
To understand why caste persists so strongly, we must look beyond its structural and political
dimensions to its role in "everyday life." Drawing on insights from recent sociological studies,
caste is not just a concept but an "affective" reality—experienced through emotions, feelings,
and bodily sensations.
Caste as Lived Experience:
•
•
•
•

The 'Caste Gaze': The subtle, often non-verbal, way individuals are "sized up" based on
their name, speech, or appearance to place them within the caste hierarchy.
The Politics of Food and Water: The persistence of separate utensils for Dalits in rural
areas or the subtle questioning of food habits are everyday manifestations of caste
boundaries.
Language and Humiliation: The use of caste-based slurs or patronizing language serves
to reinforce hierarchy and inflict psychological harm.
Embodied Identity: Caste is felt in the body as pride, shame, confidence, or fear depending
on one's social location.

This "everyday" dimension explains why caste remains a powerful force even in modern, urban
settings where overt discrimination is illegal. It is reproduced not just by structures, but by
deeply internalized feelings and practices.
Caste Among Other Religions
Though colonial and orientalist narratives have framed caste as primarily a Hindu
phenomenon, however, caste is also a feature of other religious groups.
Caste among Muslims

Caste among Sikhs

Caste continues to influence various aspects of Sikh life, including marriage, social interactions,
and religious practices. Marriage practices often reflect caste considerations. Jat Sikhs often
holding dominant positions in religious and social institutions, perpetuating systemic
discrimination.

Aspiration of a ‘Casteless Society’
The idea of a "casteless" society, where caste identity is irrelevant, is often championed as a
solution to India’s caste-based inequalities.
The term ‘casteless’ refers to the ‘absence of caste’ in society. A ‘casteless’ society is imagined
as an inclusive society that provides equal opportunity for every individual regardless of
inheritance and birth.
According to Sociologist M.N. Srinivas, caste is a complex social structure that has adapted
and changed throughout history but remains a powerful force in modern Indian society. To
him, “casteless” is a myth in the Indian context, because even though some people may claim
to be casteless or advocate for a casteless society, they are often still influenced by caste in
various ways.
•
•

Upper castes often display caste pride through surnames, marrying within their caste, and
forming caste-based associations, sometimes unconsciously due to deep-seated
socialization.
Conversely, lower castes face persistent structural obstacles in education and
employment, resulting in continued upper-caste dominance in universities and
corporations. For many lower-caste individuals, reservation policies (caste based) have
provided a pathway to education and employment opportunities that they might not have
otherwise had.

Prescription for a ‘Casteless’ Society
According to Ambedkar, apart from Education, another important step towards creating a
casteless society is inter-caste marriages.

3.2. The Salience of 'Sect' in Indian Society
A sect is a sub-group within a larger religion, emerging from differing beliefs or practices. In India,
sects are powerful social units whose influence often intersects with caste and regional
identities.
•

Interplay with Caste and Region: Sects can sometimes cut across castes, uniting followers
from different backgrounds under a single spiritual leader. However, many sects are closely
associated with specific dominant castes or regional communities, reinforcing those
identities.

Student Notes:

Case Study: The Lingayat Demand
The Lingayat community in Karnataka, traditionally
considered a sect within Hinduism, has been demanding
recognition as a separate religion. Their claim is based
on a distinct philosophy (of social equality, rejecting
Brahminical rituals and caste hierarchy) originating with
the 12th-century reformer Basavanna.
This case illustrates the complex and fluid boundaries
between sect, caste (as Lingayats have internal subdivisions), and religious identity, and how such claims
can become major political issues.

Case Study: The 'Dera' Phenomenon in North India
Sects known as Deras, particularly Dera Sacha Sauda
in Punjab and Haryana, exemplify the modern role of
sects.
•

•
•

Social Base: They draw a massive following
primarily from marginalized communities,
especially Dalits and OBCs, who feel excluded
from mainstream religious institutions dominated by upper castes (like Jat Sikhs). The
Deras offer a space of equality, dignity, and community.
Political Clout: This large, consolidated social base makes them a powerful vote bank.
During elections, political parties across the spectrum actively seek the endorsement of
the Dera head, as it can influence the outcome in dozens of constituencies.
Significance: The Dera phenomenon illustrates how modern sects can emerge to fill social
vacuums, challenge traditional caste hierarchies, and become powerful non-state actors
shaping regional politics.

4. Impact and Transformation - Family, Marriage, and
Socialization
The family and marriage are foundational institutions that have traditionally provided stability
and continuity to Indian society. However, the forces of change are leading to significant
transformations in their structure, values, and functions.

4.2. The Institution of Marriage: Tradition Meets
Modernity
The institution of marriage in India is at a
crossroads, navigating the tension between
traditional views and modern aspirations.
•

•

Is Marriage as a Sacrament Losing Value?:
Traditionally, Hindu marriage is considered a
sacrament—a sacred, indissoluble union
meant for fulfilling religious duties (dharma)
and ensuring procreation. However, modern
forces have infused contractual elements
into it.
○ Influence of Individualism: Educated,
urban individuals increasingly view
marriage as a partnership based on
emotional compatibility, companionship,
and mutual respect rather than just a
social obligation.
○ Legal Reforms: The introduction of
divorce and separation under laws like
the Hindu Marriage Act, 1955, legally
transformed the institution from an
indissoluble one to a dissolvable
contract.
○ Rising Divorce Rates: While still low
globally at around 1.1%, the divorce rate
in India has doubled over the last two
decades, reflecting changing attitudes
and
increased
female
economic
independence.
Changing Marriage Patterns:
○ Inter-caste & Inter-religious Marriages:
While social norms still favor endogamy,
there is a slow increase in
inter-caste
marriages,
recorded at 5.8% (NFHS-5).
They are more common in
urban areas due to greater
social
interaction
and
anonymity.
Inter-religious
marriages remain much rarer
and often face significant
social resistance and legal hurdles due to anti-conversion laws in several states.

Live-in relationships are reshaping the fabric of Indian society. Especially in terms of legal and
societal acceptance, their rise indicates a shift towards more personal freedom, individualism
and alternative lifestyles.

Educational Hypogamy in India
Educational hypergamy, where women marry men with higher education, has declined
globally, including in India, as women’s education levels have risen. However, educational
hypogamy (women marrying men with lower education) has sharply increased in India. In the
1970s, only about 5% of women married men with lower education, but by 2018, this figure
had risen to about 20%.
Factors Contributing to Educational Hypogamy in India:
•

•

Strong Gender Scripts: India's hierarchical gender regime often treats women’s education
differently. While women’s education has improved, societal norms still emphasize
appearance as the most important quality for women in the marriage market, whereas for
men, it’s the ability to earn a living. This leads highly educated women to marry men with
lower education but more privileged family backgrounds, prioritizing family and economic
status over educational parity.
Consanguineous Marriages: In certain regions of India, consanguineous marriages
(marriages between blood relatives) also contribute to the trend of hypogamy. These
cultural practices can limit the pool of eligible partners with equal educational attainment,
further promoting hypogamy.

5. Impact & Transformation - Culture, Regionalism, and
Globalization
The forces of change have not only altered India's social structures but have also profoundly
impacted its cultural fabric and sense of identity.

5.1. Indian Culture and Globalization: Homogenization or
Hybridization?
A central debate in Indian society is the impact
of globalization on its rich cultural diversity.
•

•

Hybridity

The Indian film industry, Bollywood, is a

argument is that globalization promotes

The Indian Reality: Glocalization and Hybridity: However, a closer look reveals that
homogenization is not the dominant trend in India. Instead, Indian society has shown a
remarkable ability to adapt, absorb, and Indianize global influences, leading to:

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

○

○

Glocalization: The process where global products and ideas are adapted to suit local
tastes and contexts. The classic example is McDonald's offering the "McAloo Tikki"
burger, specifically designed for the Indian palate.
Cultural Hybridity: The creation of new and unique cultural forms by blending global and
local elements. This is seen across various domains, from music (e.g., fusion music
blending Indian classical with Western genres) to language (the widespread use of
'Hinglish').

Losing Local for Global? A Layered Identity
The question of whether Indians are losing their local identity for a global one is not a zerosum game. Instead, globalization has led to the formation of multiple, layered identities.
•

•

Coexistence of Identities: An individual in modern India can simultaneously hold and
express multiple identities. For example, a young professional from Chennai working in
Bengaluru can be a proud Tamilian (local/regional identity), an Indian (national identity),
and a 'global citizen' or 'netizen' who consumes global media, follows international
trends, and interacts with people worldwide on the internet.
Assertion of Local Identity: Paradoxically, globalization can also lead to a conscious
assertion and revival of local identities. As people are exposed to a uniform global culture,
there is often a renewed interest in preserving and promoting one's own unique language,
traditions, and cuisine as a marker of distinction and pride.

Therefore, rather than a simple loss, we are witnessing a complex negotiation and coexistence
of identities at different scales.

5.2. The Dynamics of Regionalism

Student Notes:

Regionalism—the expression of a shared identity based on a specific geographical region—is a
powerful and persistent feature of Indian society and politics.
•
•

Bases of Regionalism: It is rooted in India's immense diversity, where regions often have a
distinct language, culture, ethnicity, and history that pre-date the formation of modern
states.
Drivers of Regional Movements: The rise of regionalism can be attributed to two key
interconnected factors:
○ Unequal Regional Development : A primary driver is the perception of economic
neglect, discrimination, or an unequal distribution of developmental benefits by the
central government. Demands for separate states or greater autonomy are often fueled
by economic backwardness.
○ Cultural Assertiveness: Regionalism is also a powerful expression of cultural
assertiveness. It reflects a desire by a community to protect and promote its distinct
language and culture against a perceived threat of domination or imposition by a larger,
more dominant culture.

Case Study: The 'Bhil Pradesh'
Demand
The recent demand for a
separate state of 'Bhil
Pradesh', carved out of areas in
Rajasthan, Gujarat, Madhya
Pradesh, and Maharashtra, is a
contemporary example of
regionalism. This demand is
driven by the dual factors of:
• Cultural Identity: It is an assertion of the distinct tribal cultural identity of the Bhil
community.
• Developmental Neglect: It is fueled by a long history of socio-economic marginalization
and the feeling that the developmental needs of the tribal population have been neglected
by the respective state governments.

5.3. Tradition, Values, and the Challenge of Obscurantism
•

Continuity of Traditional Values :
○ Indian society maintains a strong sense of continuity with its past.
○ Traditional values are passed down through generations via social institutions like the
family (through socialization), the caste system (through endogamy and social norms),
and religious practices and festivals. This ensures that even amidst rapid change, a
connection to traditional ethos remains.
Tradition vs. Reason: The Debate on Obscurantism: A critical question is whether customs
and traditions suppress reason and lead to obscurantism.
○ The Argument For: In many instances, "tradition" is invoked to justify harmful, irrational,
or discriminatory practices. Examples include superstitions leading to violence (e.g.,
witch-hunting), the justification of caste-based discrimination, or the opposition to
women's rights in the name of culture.
○ A Balanced View: However, not all tradition is obscurantist. Many traditions contain
accumulated ecological wisdom, artistic heritage, and principles that foster social
cohesion. The challenge for a modernizing society is not to reject tradition wholesale, but
to critically evaluate it through the lens of constitutional values, retaining what is just and
progressive while reforming or discarding what is oppressive and irrational.

•

6. Cross-Cutting Issues of Diversity and Inequality

Student Notes:

This final section delves into complex cross-cutting issues where India's features of diversity and
hierarchy intersect, creating unique social challenges and contemporary debates about justice
and policy.

6.1.
The
Tribal
Question:
Between
Diversity and a Single
Category
India is home to over 700
Scheduled Tribes, making them an
incredibly diverse group.
•

Acknowledging
Diversity:
Tribal
communities
vary
immensely in terms of:
○ Population: While Gonds
have a population of over
12 million, the Great
Andamanese
number
fewer than 50.
○ Linguistic Diversity: They
speak
hundreds
of
languages from distinct
linguistic families. (The
Santhals in Jharkhand
speak
Santhali
(Austroasiatic), while the
Gonds speak Gondi (Dravidian)).
○ Racial Diversity: The Gonds, Konds, and Todas belong to the Dravidian group; Bhutias
in Sikkim and Lepchas in Darjeeling belong to the Mongoloid group.
○ Social Diversity: Tribes have different social systems (The Khasi tribe in Meghalaya
follows a matrilineal system, while the Gonds and Bhils have a patrilineal system.
○ Political Diversity: Some tribes have traditional governance, others follow modern
political structures. (The Mising of Assam follow a tribal council system, while the Bodos
have a more centralized governance model.)
○ Economic Diversity: Livelihoods range from agriculture, hunting, and gathering, to
industrial labor. (Gonds and Bhils are agricultural; Tea Tribes in Assam work as
plantation laborers; Todas in the Nilgiris have a pastoral economy.)

Acknowledging this diversity is crucial to avoid stereotyping.
•

Contexts for a 'Single Category': Despite this diversity, it is analytically and administratively
valid to consider them as a single category in specific contexts:
○ Constitutional & Administrative Context: For the purpose of affirmative action
(reservations) and protective legislation like the 5th and 6th Schedules of the
Constitution and the Forest Rights Act, 2006.
○ Shared Vulnerabilities: When discussing common issues like development-led
displacement due to dams and mining projects, loss of traditional rights over jal, jangal,
zameen (water, forest, land), systemic poverty, and poor health indicators.
○ Shared Historical Experience: In their relationship with mainstream society, most tribal
communities share a history of geographical and social isolation, and a common
experience of marginalization.

Case Study: The Favorable Tribal Sex Ratio
A significant feature of tribal society is that the sex ratio in many tribal communities is more
favorable to women (990 overall) than in Scheduled Caste or dominant caste groups.
Sociologists attribute this to several factors:
•
•
•
•

Social Structure: Many tribal societies have less rigid patriarchal structures.
Value of Women: Women often play a significant role in the tribal economy, granting
them a higher social status.
Lower Dowry Prevalence: The practice of dowry is less prevalent; some communities have
the custom of 'bride price'.
Less Access to Technology: Historically, limited access to modern sex-selective
technologies has also been a factor.

6.2. The Link Between Diversity and Marginality
A critical proposition is that there is a high correlation between India's cultural diversities and its
socio-economic marginalities.
•

Evidence of Correlation:
○ Linguistic minorities: Tulu speakers in Karnataka, often face challenges in accessing
education and government services in their native language.
○ Scheduled Tribes (STs): They often experience higher poverty rates and poor socioeconomic indicators. Factors such as displacement due to development projects, limited
access to education, add to their marginalization.
○ Religious minorities: Muslims in India
suffer from many socio-economic
disadvantages such as low literacy levels
and income than national average.
○ Regional diversity: Many regions in India
such as Mountainous villages, tribal
villages in forest areas, regions such as
Bihar have low per capita income and poor
socio-economic indicators.
Is Diversity the Cause?
The correlation does not imply causation.
Diversity itself is not the cause of marginality.
The marginality arises from the social
structure of hierarchy and the politics of
neglect
or
discrimination
by
the
mainstream/state. The failure to nurture
pluralism (active appreciation of diversity)
often leads to the marginalization of minority
groups.
○ Counter-Examples:
Some
minority
communities, like the Parsis or Jains, have high socio-economic indicators,
demonstrating that being a minority does not automatically lead to marginalization.

•

Affirmative Action and its Discontents
The Premise: Affirmative action, primarily through the policy of reservations for SCs, STs, and
OBCs, is India's most significant tool for achieving social justice and rectifying historical
discrimination.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

Student Notes:

The Critique: Why Benefits Don't Reach All:
•
•
•
•

Elite Capture / 'Creamy Layer' Issue: The benefits are often cornered by the relatively
better-off sections within the reserved categories, who are better equipped to compete.
This has led to demands for excluding the 'creamy layer'.
Implementation Gaps: Issues like unfilled reserved vacancies, inadequate implementation
of welfare schemes, and subtle discrimination within institutions hinder the policy's
effectiveness.
The Private Sector Gap: With the private sector being the primary engine of job creation
post-liberalization, the absence of a reservation policy there limits the scope of affirmative
action.
Focus on Distribution, Not Foundation: Critics argue that reservations act as a distributive
mechanism but do not adequately address foundational issues like poor quality of primary
schooling and healthcare for marginalized communities, which are essential prerequisites
for them to be able to utilize reservation policies effectively.

The debate is now moving towards complementing reservations with more robust policies
aimed at strengthening the human capital of marginalized communities through targeted
scholarships, quality education, and skill development.

Glossary of Essential Concepts
This glossary provides concise definitions of key academic and feminist terms crucial for highscoring, analytical answers.
•
•
•
•
•
•

•
•

Patriarchy: A social and ideological system where men hold primary power and authority
over women in all social institutions (family, state, economy, religion). It is not just about
individual men but about a systemic structure of male dominance.
Gender vs. Sex: A foundational feminist distinction. Sex refers to biological attributes (male,
female), while Gender is a socio-cultural construct—the set of roles, norms, and behaviors
that society deems appropriate for men and women.
Gender Equality: The state of equal access to rights, resources, and opportunities for all
genders.
Gender Equity: The process of being fair to all genders. It involves using measures
(sometimes differential) to compensate for historical and social disadvantages to achieve an
equal playing field.
Women's Empowerment: The process by which women gain agency (the capacity to make
strategic life choices) and control over their lives, encompassing social, economic, and
political power.
Intersectionality : A critical framework for understanding how different social identities—
like caste, class, religion, and gender—overlap and interact to create unique and
compounded systems of discrimination. For example, a Dalit woman experiences a form of
oppression that is different from that experienced by a Savarna woman or a Dalit man.
Bodily Autonomy: The fundamental right of a person to self-governance over their own body
without coercion—encompassing choices related to health, reproduction, sexuality, and
consent.
Unpaid Care Work / Second Shift: The vast, unrecognized, and uncompensated domestic
and caregiving labor (cooking, cleaning, childcare, elder care) that is disproportionately
performed by women, constituting a "second shift" after their paid work.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

39

www.visionias.in

# 8468022022

©Vision IAS

Student Notes:

•
•

•
•
•

•
•

Glass Ceiling & Sticky Floor: Metaphors for workplace inequality. The Glass Ceiling is the
invisible barrier preventing women from reaching senior leadership positions. The Sticky
Floor refers to the forces that keep women confined to low-paying, low-mobility jobs.
Feminization of Labor / Poverty: A dual concept referring to (a) the rising proportion of
women in the workforce, and (b) the concentration of women in low-wage, insecure, and
informal jobs. Feminization of Poverty describes the trend of women experiencing poverty
at significantly higher rates than men.
Structural Violence: Harm caused not by direct physical assault, but by unjust social
structures and institutions that prevent people from meeting their basic needs. Skewed sex
ratios and high maternal mortality are examples of structural violence against women.
Son Meta-Preference: A deep-rooted cultural and economic bias for male children, which
drives practices like sex-selective abortion and the neglect of daughters.
Gender Mainstreaming & Gender Budgeting: Gender Mainstreaming is the process of
assessing the implications for all genders of any planned action, including legislation and
policies. Gender Budgeting is an application of this, analyzing a government's budget for its
differential impacts and ensuring gender commitments are translated into budgetary
commitments.
Phallocentrism: A worldview or ideology centered on the masculine perspective, where the
male is the central point of reference and the female is defined in relation to him. As seen in
many literary and religious texts.
Compulsory Heterosexuality: A term used by feminist scholars to describe the societal
assumption and enforcement of heterosexuality as the only natural or acceptable sexual
orientation, thereby marginalizing all other forms of sexual expression.

Student Notes:

Introduction

Student Notes:

The status of women in contemporary India presents a significant paradox. While our cultural
ethos has traditions of revering feminine deities and women have occupied the highest public
offices, key social indicators reveal a stark reality of deep-rooted gender inequality and
structural discrimination.
This contradiction is grounded in facts. India celebrates its female scientists and entrepreneurs,
yet grapples with a persistently low Female Labour Force Participation (FLFP) rate (around 41%
in 2024) and an adverse Child Sex Ratio (919 girls per 1000 boys, Census 2011).
To write a comprehensive answer, one must move beyond a monolithic view of "the Indian
woman." Therefore, this document adopts an intersectional approach. It examines how a
woman's lived experience is fundamentally shaped by the overlapping identities of gender,
caste, class, religion, and region—a framework essential for a nuanced societal analysis in GS
Paper 1.

1. Conceptual Frameworks for Understanding Gender
A clear grasp of foundational concepts is essential for structuring high-quality answers on
women's issues. This section clarifies these core ideas, providing the analytical tools for the
sections that follow.

1.1. Distinguishing Key Concepts for Policy and Analysis
1.1.1. Notion of ‘Gender’
Understanding women's issues requires recognizing the concept of gender.
Distinguishing Sex and Gender:
•
•

Sex: Determined by biological factors like
chromosomes and reproductive organs.
Gender: Gender refers to the socially
constructed roles, behaviours, activities, and
expectations associated with masculinity and
femininity. These expectations are not universal
and can vary across cultures and historical
periods.

The roles associated with gender are often defined
by society’s expectations, attitudes, and portrayals.
These affect personal attitudes, career choices, and
behaviors. Thus, to be male or female means that
one has to act a certain way, present oneself in a
certain manner both publicly and privately, and
perform certain responsibilities.

Gender Identity
Gender identity refers to a person’s
deeply-felt, inherent sense of being a
boy, a man, or male; a girl, a woman,
or female; or an alternative gender
(e.g., gender-queer, gender nonconforming, boygirl, ladyboy) which
may or may not correspond to a
person’s sex assigned at birth or to a
person’s primary or secondary sex
characteristics. Since gender identity
is internal, a person’s gender identity
is not necessarily visible to others.
It is debated that Self-identification is
but one form of identification. It
doesn't replace identification by
other means.

There are many factors which contribute to the
understanding of gender and gender roles. Primarily,
the agents of socialization that shape these roles
include media portrayals, religious organizations and beliefs, and parent’s beliefs and actions.

Student Notes:

•

Gender Equality: This is the end goal. It signifies a state where all genders have equal rights,
resources, and opportunities. The aim is the
sameness of opportunity and outcome.
• Gender Equity: This is the process or pathway.
It involves fairness and justice, recognizing that
historical disadvantages mean different groups
may need different, targeted interventions
(affirmative action) to create a level playing
field.
○ Example: A company hiring more women in
a male-dominated field is an equity measure
to achieve long-term equality.
• Women’s Empowerment: This is the process of
gaining power and agency. It is about women
acquiring the capacity to make strategic life
choices and act on them, covering economic,
social, and political spheres.

This system is perpetuated through Gender Socialization, the lifelong process of learning socially

•

1.1.4. The Essential Analytical Lens: Intersectionality
Intersectionality is a powerful tool for Mains answers to add depth and avoid generalization. It
means analyzing how different identities like caste, class, religion, and gender overlap to create
unique experiences of discrimination.
For example, a government scheme promoting digital banking might succeed with an urban
middle-class woman. However, it may fail to reach a rural, landless woman from a lower caste.
Her barriers are not just gender, but a combination of poverty (cannot afford a smartphone),
caste location (may not be able to enter the bank freely), and lack of digital literacy.
Acknowledging this intersectionality is key to effective policy design.
Case Study: The Intersectional Vulnerability of Adivasi Women
Adivasi (tribal) women in India face a unique form of compounded marginalization. Their
challenges are an outcome of the intersection of:
•
•
•
•

Gender: Facing patriarchal norms within their communities and from mainstream society.
Ethnicity (Adivasi Identity): Facing discrimination, cultural alienation, and loss of
traditional rights.
Class (Poverty): Being among the most economically impoverished groups.
Location: Residing in resource-rich regions often targeted for development projects.

This intersection makes them uniquely vulnerable to displacement by development projects
(dams, mining), loss of control over forest resources. Their struggles are often invisible to both
mainstream feminist movements and tribal rights movements that may be male-dominated.

Case Study: The Paradox of Skewed Sex Ratios in Prosperous States
States like Punjab and Haryana, despite high per capita incomes, have historically reported
some of India's worst child sex ratios. This paradox is explained by the fact that prosperity
provides easier access to sex-selective technology (like ultrasound), which is then used to act
on persistent patriarchal norms of son preference and concerns over dowry. This shows
economic growth alone cannot erase deep-seated gender bias.

Social and cultural norms often create the most formidable barriers to women's freedom and
development.
•

•

Barriers to Education: Despite progress in female literacy (65.46%, Census 2011), significant
barriers persist.
○ Barriers include social norms prioritizing boys' education, the burden of domestic chores,
safety concerns during commute, and inadequate sanitation facilities in schools.
Gender-Based Violence (GBV): Gender-based violence (GBV) or violence against women
and girls (VAWG), is a global pandemic that affects 1 in 3 women in their lifetime. It
presents in many forms:

some form of harassment in public spaces.

CYBER HARASSMENT AND CRIMES
The internet, a vast digital landscape offering connection and opportunity, can also become a
breeding ground for crime. Women in India are disproportionately targeted by cybercriminals,
facing a range of online threats that exploit technology to violate their privacy, safety, and
dignity.
The Scope of the Problem
•

Prevalence: According to NCRB, in 2021, over 20% of all reported cybercrimes (52,974)
targeted women.

Impact: These crimes can have devastating consequences for victims. They can lead to
emotional distress, social isolation, and even physical harm. The fear of online harassment
can also limit women's freedom of expression and participation in the digital space.

Perpetuation of Online Harassment:
•
•
•

Anonymity: The ability to remain anonymous online emboldens perpetrators to engage in

Social Media Algorithms: Platforms often prioritize sensational content, which can

Cultural Attitudes: Societal norms that condone or overlook sexism contribute to a

Decoding Structural Violence Against Women
Concept: The term "Structural Violence," unlike direct physical violence, refers to the harm and
suffering caused by social structures, systems, and institutions that prevent people from
meeting their basic needs. It is often invisible, indirect, and normalized. Patriarchy itself is a
system of structural violence.
Manifestations in India:
•
•
•
•

Demographic Violence: Skewed sex ratios resulting from son meta-preference are not
individual choices but outcomes of a social structure that devalues females. This is a form
of slow, systemic violence.
Institutional Violence: Unequal access to food, healthcare, and education for girls is a form
of violence perpetrated by societal norms and family structures.
Economic Violence: Denying women property rights, paying them less for equal work
(gender pay gap), and devaluing their unpaid care work are forms of economic violence
that create dependency and vulnerability.
Symbolic Violence: The normalization of stereotypes and sexist language in media and daily
life perpetuates patriarchal ideology and justifies discrimination.

Significance: Viewing women's issues through the lens of structural violence helps shift the
focus from blaming individual perpetrators to analyzing and challenging the unjust social,
economic, and political systems that cause widespread, predictable harm to women.

3. Women, Work, and Economic Arenas
The economic dimension is central to women's empowerment. This section examines women's
engagement with the Indian economy, analyzing the paradoxes of participation, the impact of
major economic transformations, and the nature of emerging work opportunities.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

Student Notes:

3.1. Female Labour Force Participation (FLFP): Trends and
Paradoxes
•

Current Status & Trends: India has seen a notable increase in its Female Labour Force
Participation (FLFP), reaching approximately 41.7% in 2023-24, up from 33% in 2021-22. This
closes the gap with the global average (around 47%) but a substantial gap remains compared
to men's participation.

The Feminization Hypothesis or the U-shaped theory of Female Labor Force Participation
Rate in India

Claudia Goldin (Nobel laureate 2023), suggests a U-shaped relationship between economic
development and female labor force participation (FLFPR).
•

Initially, as family income rises from low levels, women may withdraw from the workforce
(income effect) to focus on domestic duties, which is often seen as a marker of social
status.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

48

www.visionias.in

# 8468022022

©Vision IAS

•

At higher levels of development and education, women tend to re-enter the workforce in
higher-skilled, better-paying jobs (substitution effect). This helps explain why rising
incomes don't always translate to higher FLFP.

Educational attainment and FLFPR
•
•
•

Women with lower levels of education often enter the workforce out of necessity, filling
roles in informal or lower-wage sectors.
At the opposite end, women with advanced degrees secure professional careers, resulting
in higher LFPR at both low and high ends of educational attainment.
This curve reveals a dip in workforce participation at the mid-level education (secondary
level) but a rise as women achieve higher qualifications.

Investing in higher education for women, therefore, emerges as a strategic approach to
unlocking the full potential of the female workforce.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

3.2. The World of Work: Formal, Informal, and the Burden of Care
•

•

•

Informal Sector Dominance: A staggering 90% of working women in India are engaged in the
informal sector—as agricultural laborers, domestic workers, street vendors, or home-based
workers.
○ This work is characterized by low wages, precarious conditions, and a lack of social
security, making women highly vulnerable and contributing to the "feminisation of
poverty".
Challenges in the Formal Sector:
○ The Glass Ceiling & Sticky Floor: Women face invisible barriers (glass ceiling) preventing
their rise to senior leadership. Data suggests women hold only 15-20% of leadership
positions in Indian companies. Many also get stuck in low-level jobs with limited
mobility (sticky floor).
○ Gender Pay Gap: A significant pay gap persists. The World Economic Forum has noted
that for every dollar a man gets paid, a woman in India is, on average, paid only 54 cents.
○ Workplace Stress: Reports indicate that women in professions like auditing and IT often
work over 55 hours per week, with a high percentage of workers reporting that
workplace stress negatively impacts their mental health.
The Economics of Unpaid Care
Work: The
disproportionate
burden of unpaid care work is the
single biggest barrier to women's
economic participation.
○ Indian women perform about
8.4 times the amount of
unpaid work compared to
men, which, if valued, would
contribute 15% to 17% of
India's GDP. This "dual
burden" or "second shift"
leads to severe time poverty,
limiting women's ability to pursue education, leisure, and paid employment.

The Care Economy: Making the Invisible Visible
Concept: The Care Economy encompasses the vast spectrum of paid and unpaid work
dedicated to caring for others—including childcare, elder care, and domestic household tasks.
For long, the unpaid component of this economy, overwhelmingly performed by women, has
been invisible in national economic
accounting.
The ILO's 5R Framework for Action: To
create a gender-equal care economy,
the International Labour Organization
(ILO) advocates a 5R framework:
1. Recognition: Formally recognizing
the immense economic and social
value of unpaid care work.
2. Reduction: Reducing the drudgery
of care work through better
infrastructure (piped water, clean
fuel).

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

50

www.visionias.in

# 8468022022

©Vision IAS

Student Notes:

3. Redistribution: Promoting more equitable sharing of care work between men and women,
and between families and the state.
4. Rewarding: Ensuring decent work, fair pay, and social protection for paid care workers.
5. Representation: Ensuring care workers have a voice in policymaking.

Significance: Investing in the care economy—through public services like affordable childcare
and elder care—is not just a social welfare measure. The Economic Survey 2024 estimates that
a public investment of 2% of GDP in the care sector could generate 11 million jobs, nearly 70%
of which would benefit women. It is a powerful tool for boosting FLFP, creating jobs, and
achieving gender equality.

empowerment.

4. Agency, Movements, and Political Participation
Despite facing structural barriers, women in India have not been passive victims. They have
consistently exercised their agency, individually and collectively. This section explores the role of
women's movements, organizations, and their engagement with the political sphere.

4.1. Women's Movements in India: Contributions and Critiques
The women's movement in India has a rich history, evolving through distinct phases.

4.1.1. Historical Overview:
Phase 1: Reform and Anti-Colonial Struggles (Late 19th - Mid 20th Century)
Reformist Movements
•
•
•

Savitribai Phule and Ishwar Chandra Vidyasagar spearheaded campaigns against Sati (widow
burning) and child marriage.
Educational Empowerment: Jyotirao Phule established the first girls' school in India in 1848.
Key Organisations:
○ Brahmo Samaj: Founded by Raja Rammohun Roy, fought for the abolition of Sati.
○ Arya Samaj: Championed women's empowerment.

Anti-Colonial Struggles
•
•

Integration with Independence Movement: Women played a crucial role in the Indian
independence movement, with Mahatma Gandhi mobilizing them for swaraj (self-rule).
Significant Organisations:
○ All India Women's Conference (1927): Advocated for political participation and social
reforms.
○ Women’s Indian Association (1917): Focused on women’s suffrage, education, and
social reform.

Pre-independence movements, largely led by men, focused on social reforms to "uplift" women.
However, post-independence, the baton was passed on to women themselves.
Phase 2: The Quest for Legal and Social Equality (1960s - 1980s)
1. Legal and Social Reforms
• Towards Equality Report (1974): Exposed the stark realities of women's lives,
highlighting issues like declining sex ratio, economic marginalization, and discriminatory
personal laws. This served as a catalyst for further action.
○ Vina Mazumdar and Lotika Sarkar, played a key role in compiling this
groundbreaking report.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

52

www.visionias.in

# 8468022022

©Vision IAS

2. The Rise of Autonomous Women's Groups (1970s-1980s): The 1970s and 80s saw a surge
of autonomous women's groups, independent from state control. These groups tackled a
diverse range of issues critical to women's empowerment:
• Economic Empowerment: Pioneering organizations like the Self-Employed Women's
Association (SEWA), championed the cause of women workers in the informal sector,
providing them with training, skill development, and access to credit.
• Legislative Reforms: Women's organizations actively lobbied the state for legislative
reforms.
○ The All India Democratic Women's Association (AIDWA), for instance, played a
crucial role in advocating for women's political rights and equal opportunities.
• Legal Support: Organizations like the All India Muslim Women's Personal Law Board
(AIMWPLB) and Bhartiya Muslim Mahila Andolan (BMMA) provided vital legal aid. They
also provided support to Muslim women facing issues related to marriage, divorce,
property rights, and child custody.
○ The landmark Shah Bano case, where a Muslim woman fought for maintenance after
divorce, is a prime example of their work.
3. Cooperative movements: Cooperative societies like Shri Mahila Griha Udyog (Lijjat Papad)
not only provided women with employment opportunities but also empowered them
through collective ownership and decision-making.
Phase 3: Decentralization and Grassroots Activism (1990s - Early 2000s)
Grassroots Movements: The 1990s witnessed a shift towards decentralization and grassroots
activism.
•
•

Constitutional Amendments: The 73rd and 74th amendments (1992) mandated 33%
reservation for women in Panchayats and Municipalities.
NGO Rise: Women-led NGOs provided support networks and legal aid.

Diverse Movements
•

Dalit and Marginalized Women: This phase also gave rise to movements addressing the
specific needs of Dalit women and other marginalized communities. These movements
challenged the notion of a singular "womanhood" and highlighted the intersectionalities of
gender, caste, and class.

Digital Feminism: The advent of the digital age ushered in a new era of activism. Social media
platforms like Twitter became fertile ground for movements like #MeToo, which highlighted
issues of sexual harassment and abuse.
•

The online space broadened the agenda of the women's movement, bringing issues of
LGBTQ+ rights, body autonomy, and mental health to the forefront.

Contemporary Organisations
•
•

Peace Movements: Organisations like Naga Mothers Association addressed problems of
conflict, peace-building, and substance abuse in insurgency-affected areas.
Environmental Advocacy: Ecofeminism linked women's development with environmental
protection.

4.1.2. Problems Faced by Women's Organisations
Structural Challenges
•
•
•

Unorganized Structure: Many women's NGOs lack proper organization.
Financial Constraints: Limited funding hampers their activities.
Urban-Centric Focus: Most organisations serve urban women, neglecting rural issues.

Social and Political Issues
•
•
•

Narrow Support Base: Women-centric issues often lack active support from men.
State Dependence: Many organisations, despite claiming autonomy, align closely with state
agendas.
Fragmentation: There is significant fragmentation among women's organisations, with
different sections constituting their own movements.

4.1.3. Critiques of the Movement:
•

•

Urban and Middle-Class Bias: A significant critique is that the mainstream women's
movement has often been dominated by urban, educated, middle-class women, focusing
on issues more pertinent to their lives while sometimes failing to adequately represent the
concerns of women from rural and lower socio-economic strata.
Lack of Intersectionality: It has been criticized for not fully addressing the unique and
compounded discrimination faced by Dalit, Adivasi, and minority women, whose struggles
are shaped by the intersection of caste, class, and gender.

4.2. Landmark Judgements Related to Women Issues
4.2.1. Women’s Rights to Privacy and Dignity
•
•

State of Maharashtra Vs Madhukar Narayan Mardikar (1991): A police inspector accused of
attempted rape of a woman of ‘easy’ virtue. The Supreme Court held: “Even a woman of
easy virtue is entitled to privacy and no one can invade her privacy.”
Joseph Shine v. Union of India (2018): The Supreme Court held that “The law in adultery is
a codified rule of patriarchy. Legal sovereignty of one sex over another is wrong. Section 497
of IPC (Adultery) violated a woman's right to dignity, resulting in infringement of Article 21
of the Constitution.”

4.2.2. Women’s Rights in the Workplace and Employment
•
•

Vishaka and Others v. State of Rajasthan and Others (1997): Supreme Court held that sexual
harassment of women at the workplace violated the fundamental rights of working women
under Articles 14, 15, and 21 of the Constitution.
Babita Puniya case (2020): Supreme Court held that “Permanent Commission should be
granted to women in the Army regardless of their service, in all ten streams where the
Central government has already taken a decision to grant Short Service Commission to
women.”

4.2.3. Reproductive Rights and Personal Liberty
•
•

Suchita Srivastava case (2009): Right to reproductive choice is an integral part of a woman's
right to life and personal liberty under Article 21 of the Constitution.
The Principle Secretary H&FW case (2023): Supreme Court held that “Even unmarried
women who become pregnant from consensual sexual relationships are entitled to
terminate pregnancy up to 24 weeks.”

4.2.4. Marriage, Family, and Personal Choice
•

•

Lata Singh case (2006): Supreme Court held that “Everyone has the right to marry and a
definite right to choose their life partner under the ambit of Article 21 of the Constitution. It
ordered that the police initiate criminal action against people who commit violence against
those who decide on inter-religious or inter-caste marriages.”
Sabrimala Temple Entry Case (2018): Sabarimala Temple's custom of barring women of
menstruating age (10-50 years) from entering is discriminatory and a violation of
fundamental rights and thus unconstitutional.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI
