4.2.5. Relating to Maternity Leave
•

Student Notes:

Deepika Singh Case (2022): Supreme Court held that “A woman's statutory right to avail
maternity leave cannot be taken away for the reason that she had availed child care leave
earlier for her non-biological kids.”

4.3. The Power of the Collective: Women's Organisations
•

•

Self-Help Groups (SHGs): SHGs have emerged
as one of the most powerful instruments for
grassroots empowerment. Beyond providing
access to micro-credit, they have become
platforms for building social capital, fostering
leadership skills, and taking collective action
on local issues like alcoholism, domestic
violence, and school infrastructure.
Trade Unions and Grassroots Groups:
Organizations have been vital in mobilizing
women.
○ Trade Unions: Groups like SEWA have
organized women in the informal sector,
providing them with bargaining power
and social security.
○ Advocacy & Peace Groups: Organizations
like the All India Democratic Women's
Association (AIDWA) have consistently
advocated for legislative reforms, while
groups like the Naga Mothers'
Association have played a crucial role in
peace-building in conflict-ridden areas.

Case Study: The SEWA Model:
Organizing the "Poor but So Many"
Founded by Ela Bhatt, the Self-Employed
Women’s Association (SEWA) is a
globally recognized trade union with a
membership of over 2.9 million women
in the informal sector. Drawing on
Gandhian principles, SEWA's unique
model combines the struggle of trade
unionism with the constructive action of
developmental programs.
It has successfully provided members
with access to finance (through its own
cooperative bank), healthcare, childcare,
and insurance, demonstrating a
powerful, scalable model for the
economic empowerment of the most
vulnerable working women.

The Feminist Principle: "The Personal is Political"
The Concept: This powerful slogan, central to
second-wave feminism, argues that women's
personal experiences are not isolated,
individual troubles but are deeply intertwined
with and caused by broader public structures of
power and patriarchy.
Breaking the Public/Private Divide:
•

•

Patriarchy has historically confined
women's issues (like domestic abuse,
marital rape, unequal housework) to the
'private' sphere of the family, deeming
them outside the scope of law and political justice.
The women's movement challenged this by asserting that what happens inside the home
is a political issue, shaped by societal power relations. For example, domestic violence is
not a "family dispute" but a crime and a violation of human rights that the state has an
obligation to address.

Relevance: This framework is fundamental to understanding the struggle for virtually all major
laws concerning women in India, from the Protection of Women from Domestic Violence Act
to the ongoing debate around criminalizing marital rape. It provides the justification for state
intervention in the so-called 'private' sphere to ensure justice and equality.

Case Study: Successful models for women’s reservation in political system
Rwanda's "Zipper Method":
•
•

Rwanda has the highest percentage of women parliamentarians globally (over 60%).
This success is largely attributed to their unique quota system – the "zipper method."
Political parties are required to alternate male and female candidates on their electoral
lists, ensuring a minimum of 30% female representation.

Bolivia's Parity Law:
•

In 2009, Bolivia enacted a law mandating equal participation of women in all elected bodies.
This law, combined with quotas for indigenous women, led to a dramatic rise in female
representation in parliament, from 13% to 50% within a decade. The law also stipulated
state funding for women candidates, addressing financial barriers to entry.

5. The State, Law, and the Path Forward
The journey towards gender equality requires proactive interventions from the state, responsive
legal frameworks, and a forward-looking approach to contemporary challenges. This module
examines the role of the state and policy, and charts a course for the future.

5.1. The Role of the State: Legal and Constitutional Frameworks
The Indian state is mandated by the Constitution to ensure gender justice.
•

•

Constitutional Provisions: The Constitution provides a strong foundation for women's rights
through articles ensuring equality before law (Art 14), prohibiting discrimination on grounds
of sex (Art 15(1)), empowering the state to make special provisions for women (Art 15(3)),
ensuring equality of opportunity in public employment (Art 16), and guaranteeing the right
to life and dignity (Art 21). Directive Principles also mandate humane conditions of work and
maternity relief (Art 42).

5.2. Policy in Practice: Gender Mainstreaming and Major Schemes
•

•

Why Gender Concerns Matter in Policy: Gender-neutral policies often end up reinforcing
existing inequalities because they fail to account for the different social realities, needs, and
barriers faced by women. Gender-responsive planning is crucial because it leads to more
effective and equitable outcomes.
○ Example: Women Panchayat leaders have been shown to prioritize investments in
public goods critical to community well-being, like drinking water and sanitation,
demonstrating how women's participation improves policy outcomes for all.
Gender Mainstreaming and Budgeting: Gender Mainstreaming is the process of integrating
a gender perspective into all stages of policy-making and implementation. A key tool for this
is Gender-Responsive Budgeting (GRB), which analyzes the government's budget for its
differential impacts on women.
○ The share of the Gender Budget in the total Union Budget has increased, reaching 8.8%
in the 2025-26 budget proposal.

5.3. Major Schemes Related to Women
5.3.1. Mission Shakti: Umbrella Scheme for Women Empowerment
‘Mission Shakti’, an Integrated Women Empowerment Programme, as Umbrella Scheme for the
Safety, Security and Empowerment of Women. This initiative has been instrumental in
improving the lives of women across the country through its two main verticals: Sambal (for
safety and security) and Samarthya (for empowerment).
1. Sambal vertical:
• One Stop Centres: It provides integrated support and assistance under one roof to
women affected by violence and those in distress, both in private and public spaces. It
provides services like medical aid, legal aid and advice, temporary shelter, police
assistance and psycho-social counselling to needy women.
• Women Helpline: It provides 24x7x365
emergency
and
non-emergency
response through telephonic short-code
181 to women, both in public and private
spaces by linking them with appropriate
authorities such as Police, One Stop
Centres, Hospitals, Legal Services
Authorities etc. Additionally, it provides
information about women welfare
schemes and programs.
• BBBP: It aims to prevent gender biased
sex selective elimination, ensure survival
and protection of girl child and also to
ensure education of the girl child.
• Nari Aadalat: It serves as an alternate Grievance Redressal Mechanism for resolving
cases of petty nature (harassment, subversion, curtailment of rights or entitlements)
faced by women at Gram panchayat level by negotiation, mediation, and reconciliation
with mutual consent for speedy, accessible, and affordable justice.
2. Samarthya Vertical
• Shakti Sadan Scheme: It is an Integrated Relief and Rehabilitation Home for women in
distressful situations including trafficked women.
• Sakhi Niwas: It aims to provide safe, secure, conveniently located, and affordable
accommodation for women who are in the workforce and/or aspire to join the
workforce. The scheme also makes a provision of Day Care Centre for children of the
residents of the Sakhi Niwas.

Palna: Its objective is to provide quality creche facility in safe and secure environment

The Pradhan Mantri Matru Vandana Yojana (PMMVY): It provides financial

5.3.2. Other Schemes for Women empowerment
Economic Empowerment
•
•
•
•
•

Gender Budget: The Gender Budget in 2025-2026 constitutes 8.86% of the total Union
budget, increasing from 6.8% in FY 2024-25.
PM Jan Dhan Yojana: 55.6% of account holders are women.
Deendayal Antyodaya Yojana-NRLM: Empowers women through Self-Help Groups (SHGs).
Entrepreneurship: High female participation in PM Mudra Yojana and Stand-Up India.
PM AWAS Yojana mandates female ownership of houses to promote gender equity.

Education and Skilling
•

•

Jan Shikshan Sansthan (JSS) Scheme: Women constitute about 82% of the total
beneficiaries.
Women in Science and Engineering (WISE KIRAN) boosts female involvement in STEM fields.
Initiatives like Vigyan Jyoti and exclusive research grants for women scientists under CSIRASPIRE aim to enhance women's participation in scientific research.

Empowerment through access to basic needs
•
•
•

Swachh Bharat Mission: Construction of toilets.
Ujjwala Yojana: Provision of clean cooking gas connections.
Jal Jeevan Mission: Provision of tap drinking water connections.

These initiatives reduce women's drudgery and care burden, freeing up time for productive work.
Political Empowerment
•
•

Nari Shakti Vandan Adhiniyam, 2023 aims to increase women's political participation.
Reservation for women in local bodies has led to greater investment in public goods related
to women’s concerns and improved child health and education outcomes.

5.3.3. A Critical Look at Major Schemes:
•
•
•

Beti Bachao, Beti Padhao (BBBP): Credited with contributing to an improved Sex Ratio at
Birth (SRB) and promoting girls' education.
PM Matru Vandana Yojana (PMMVY): A maternity benefit scheme that has faced challenges
of declining coverage (from 36% in 2019-20 to 9% in 2023-24) and conditionalities that
exclude many women.
Ujjwala Yojana: Aims to reduce the drudgery of rural women by providing clean cooking fuel
(LPG), with significant health and time-saving benefits.

5.4. Contemporary Frontiers and the Path Forward
•

The Gendered Impact of Climate Change: The climate crisis is not gender-neutral. Women,
especially poor rural women, are disproportionately affected due to their roles in securing
water, food, and fuel. The ecofeminist perspective highlights how the destruction of nature
and the marginalization of women are interconnected processes.
○ Data Point: Women and children are 14 times more likely than men to die in a climaterelated disaster. Increased heatwaves translate to more hours of unpaid care work for
women in India.

Empowerment and Population Stabilization: There is a direct and established correlation
between women's empowerment and demographic outcomes. Women's access to
education, economic agency, and control over their reproductive health are the most
effective "contraceptives." Empowering women is thus the key to achieving sustainable
population growth.

5.5. The Way Forward - A Five-Pronged Strategy
Achieving genuine gender equality in India requires a holistic, multi-pronged approach that goes
beyond piecemeal interventions. The path forward can be summarized in a five-pronged
strategy:
•

•

•

•

•

Social & Behavioral Change (Mindsets):
○ Using education, media, and community engagement to challenge deep-rooted
patriarchal norms and gender stereotypes. Engaging men and boys is critical to this
process.
Economic Empowerment (Livelihoods):
○ Increasing FLFP by creating safe and decent work opportunities, ensuring equal pay, and
investing in the care economy to reduce the burden of unpaid care work. Promoting
women's entrepreneurship and property rights is key.
Political Representation (Leadership):
○ Ensuring effective implementation of women's reservation in local bodies and
Parliament (Nari Shakti Vandan Adhiniyam) to bring women's voices to the center of
decision-making.
Legal Efficacy (Justice):
○ Strengthening the implementation of existing laws against gender-based violence. This
requires sensitizing police and judiciary, establishing fast-track courts, and ensuring
survivor-centric justice.
Human Capital Development (Capabilities):
○ Investing in women's health (nutrition, reproductive health, mental well-being) and
education at all levels, ensuring every girl has the capability to reach her full potential.

This integrated strategy recognizes that women's empowerment is not just a goal in itself, but
the foundational pillar for building a more just, prosperous, and sustainable India.

CHAPTER 3: POPULATION AND ASSOCIATED ISSUES
Key Data & Facts at a Glance
•

•

•

•

•

Population Size & Growth:
○ Current Population: India became the world's most populous nation in 2023, with over
1.428 billion people (UNFPA).
○ Decadal Growth Rate: Was 17.7% during 2001-2011 (Census 2011), showing a decline
from the previous decade, indicating a slowing growth rate.
○ Population Density: 382 persons per sq. km (Census 2011).
Fertility & Mortality:
○ Total Fertility Rate (TFR): Has fallen to 2.0 (NFHS-5, 2019-21), which is below the
replacement level of 2.1. This signifies that India has achieved population stabilization
at a national level.
○ Maternal Mortality Ratio (MMR): Has declined significantly to 97 deaths per 100,000
live births (SRS, 2018-20).
○ Infant Mortality Rate (IMR): Stands at 35.2 deaths per 1,000 live births (SRS, 2022).
○ Life Expectancy at Birth: Is approximately 67.7 years (NITI Aayog).
Age Structure & Demographic Dividend:
○ Median Age: India has a young population with a median age of around 28 years,
compared to 38 for China and 48 for Japan.
○ Youth Population (15-29 years): Constitutes about 27% of the population, forming the
core of the demographic dividend.
○ Elderly Population (60+ years): Is currently around 10% but is projected to rise to nearly
20% by 2050, indicating a rapidly aging society.
Gender Demographics:
○ Sex Ratio (Overall): Reached 1020 females per 1000 males (NFHS-5), a major
improvement.
○ Sex Ratio at Birth (SRB): Is 929 females per 1000 males (NFHS-5), still indicating the
persistence of son preference.
○ Anemia in Women: 57% of women aged 15-49 are anemic (NFHS-5).
Regional Disparities (NFHS-5):
○ TFR Variation: States like Bihar (2.98), Meghalaya (2.91), and Uttar Pradesh (2.35) have
TFRs well above replacement level, while states like Kerala (1.8) and Tamil Nadu (1.8)
have much lower rates, highlighting a stark demographic divide.

Glossary of Essential Demographic & Sociological Concepts
•
•

•

•

Demographic Transition: The theory explaining the historical shift of a country's population
from high birth/death rates to low birth/death rates, typically in stages as it develops. India
is considered to be in the late third stage of this transition.
Demographic Dividend: The economic growth potential that can result from shifts in a
population’s age structure, mainly when the share of the working-age population (15 to 64)
is larger than the non-working-age share of the population (14 and younger, and 65 and
older).
Demographic Winter : An advanced demographic stage characterized by a combination of
extremely low fertility rates (well below replacement level), a rapidly aging population, a
shrinking workforce, and a potential long-term decline in the overall population, leading to
severe economic and social challenges.
Total Fertility Rate (TFR): The average number of children a woman would have over her
childbearing years (15-49). Replacement Level TFR is 2.1, the rate at which a population
exactly replaces itself from one generation to the next, assuming no migration.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

62

www.visionias.in

# 8468022022

©Vision IAS

Student Notes:

•
•
•
•
•

Infant Mortality Rate (IMR): The number of deaths of infants under one year of age per
1,000 live births. It is a key indicator of a society's overall health status.
Maternal Mortality Ratio (MMR): The number of maternal deaths during a given time period
per 100,000 live births during the same timeperiod. It reflects the quality of a society's
maternal healthcare.
Population Momentum: The tendency for a population to continue growing even after
fertility rates have fallen to replacement level. This happens because of the large number of
people already in the childbearing age group.
Dependency Ratio: The ratio of dependents (people younger than 15 or older than 64) to
the working-age population (those 15-64). A low dependency ratio is a key component of the
demographic dividend.
Population Pyramid: A graphical illustration that shows the distribution of various age
groups in a population, which forms the shape of a pyramid when the population is growing.
The shape reveals the age-sex structure and future trends.

1. Population
Measurement

India has overtaken China to become the world's most populous country, according to UN
population estimates. Factors like census data and birth and death rates inform this milestone,
with India's population reaching 1,425,775,850, marking a pivotal moment in global
demographics.

Home Affairs (MHA) in June 2025.
Importance of Census:
○ Policy Making: Provides data for planning welfare schemes and allocating resources.
○ Political Representation: Data is used for the delimitation of parliamentary and
assembly constituencies.
○ Understanding Society: It is the most credible source of information on the size,
distribution, and composition of India's diverse population.

Consequences of a Delayed Census
• Deprivation of Entitlements: For example, Delays in updating data might exclude
approximately 12 crore additional people from accessing subsidized food grains.

•

Resource Allocation Issues: Central government funds to states are often allocated based
on population figures. Delayed census data can lead to inequitable distribution of
resources.

The Debate on a Caste-Based Census
A caste census is a systematic collection of data on individuals’ caste identities during a
nationwide population census.

There was a persistent political and social demand for a comprehensive caste-based census in
India. The Government of India has officially approved the inclusion of caste enumeration in
the upcoming national census.
Arguments in Favor:
•
•
•
•

Accurate Data for Policy Formulation: It provides updated and reliable data for effective
policy-making, addressing the gap in caste data for OBCs and other caste groups.
Reshaping Affirmative Action: Helps reassess reservation quotas and affirmative action,
ensuring equitable distribution of benefits.
Sub-categorization of OBCs: Enables sub-categorization within OBCs as recommended by
the Rohini Commission (2017), enhancing fairness in reservation distribution.
Political Representation and Inclusivity: Improves political representation of
marginalized groups and helps create more inclusive, equitable policies.

Against against:
•
•
•
•

Reinforcing Caste Identities: It may entrench caste consciousness, perpetuating divisions
and contradicting the constitutional goal of promoting fraternity and equality.
Risk of Political Exploitation: Vote-bank politics could arise, leading to demands for
reservations from politically dominant groups, increasing pressures on quotas.
Equity vs. Equality Issues: Micro-quota fragmentation could harm social cohesion and
disproportionately exclude minorities within backward classes.
Constitutional Ambiguities: No constitutional mandate for caste enumeration, raising
legal issues regarding proportional representation and the reservation system.

Suggestions for Accurate Census:
•

Standardized Caste List: Develop a unified caste list across states to avoid inconsistencies
and classification errors.
• Data Verification and Grievance Redressal: Incorporate Aadhaar integration and
establish a robust grievance redressal system to ensure accurate identity verification and
minimize errors.
• AI for Sorting and Analysis: Leverage Artificial Intelligence (AI) and Machine Learning
(ML) to streamline data sorting and classification.
A caste census can significantly improve representation and policy targeting for marginalized
communities. However, it must be executed with strong safeguards to prevent political misuse
and ensure accurate data.

1.2. Key Determinants of Population Change

Student Notes:

Population size and composition are determined by three fundamental processes.
•

Fertility: Refers to the actual reproductive performance of a population. It is influenced by
numerous social factors, including:
○ Female education and empowerment
○ Age of marriage
○ Poverty and economic status
○ Access to and use of contraception’

Declining Fertility in India
According to NFHS-5, India's Total Fertility Rate (TFR) is 2.0 children per woman, which is a
decline from 2.2 in NFHS-4 (2015-16). This suggests a slowdown in population growth.
•

Declining Fertility in India
India's fertility rate (average number of children a woman has in her lifetime) has been
steadily declining in recent decades. Here's a breakdown of key factors contributing to
this trend:
o Socio-economic Factors:
> Increased Education, Particularly for Women: According to NFHS-5, women with
higher education have a lower Total Fertility Rate (TFR). Education empowers
women, allowing them to pursue careers and delay marriage and childbearing.
> In Kerala, a state with high female literacy (92.1%), the TFR is 1.8, well below the
national replacement level.
> Improved Healthcare and Child Survival Rates: As infant and child mortality rates
decline, couples feel less compelled to have more children to ensure some survive
to adulthood. This reduces the perceived need for large families.
✓ India's under-five mortality rate has significantly decreased from 127 per
1,000 live births in 1990 to 37 per 1,000 in 2020.
> Urbanization and Cost of Living: Urban life is often expensive, with high housing
and education costs. Raising children in cities can be financially burdensome,
leading couples to opt for smaller families.
✓ For instance, Maharashtra, a highly urbanized state, has a TFR of 1.4, while
Bihar, a less urbanized state, has a TFR of 3.0.
o Socio-cultural Factors:
> Shifting Gender Norms and Women's Empowerment: As women gain more
education and employment opportunities, traditional gender roles are challenged.
Women may prioritize careers and personal aspirations before starting a family.
> Changing Family Structure: The traditional joint family system, where multiple
generations live together, is becoming less common. Nuclear families are more
prevalent, and with fewer family members to help with childcare, couples may opt
for fewer children.

•

Mortality: Mortality refers to the occurrence of death within a population. It's often studied
through mortality rates, which measure the frequency of death in a specific population over
a specific period.
•
•

Crude Death Rate (CDR): The most basic measure, it calculates the total number of deaths
per 1,000 people in a population over a year.
Infant Mortality Rate (IMR): The number of deaths of infants under one year old per 1,000
live births in a given year. This is a sensitive indicator of a population's health and wellbeing.
o 28 in 2020 as per Sample registration survey (SRS).

•

•

Maternal Mortality Rate (MMR): The number of deaths of women from pregnancyrelated complications per 100,000 live births.
o 97 in 2020 as per the SRS

Migration: Refers to the movement of people across regions. Internal migration (within the
country) and international migration do not change the total population of the world but
are major factors in changing the size and composition of a country's or region's population.

1.3. Important Theories of Population Change
•

•

Student Notes:

The Malthusian Theory: Proposed by Thomas Malthus, this theory argues that population,
when unchecked, grows at a geometric rate (1, 2, 4, 8...), while the food supply increases at
an arithmetic rate (1, 2, 3, 4...). This imbalance leads to overpopulation, which is then
corrected by "positive checks" (famine, disease, war) or "preventive checks" (moral
restraint, late marriage).
The Theory of Demographic Transition: This is the most widely accepted theory explaining
population change. It describes a sequential process of shifting from high birth and death
rates to low birth and death rates as a country develops from a pre-industrial to an
industrialized economic system. The theory outlines distinct stages that countries typically
pass through.

The Demographic Transition Model and India's 'Demographic Divide'
The Demographic Transition Model (DTM) provides a powerful lens to understand India's
complex population story. India as a whole is in the late third stage, but its states are spread
across different stages, creating a significant 'demographic divide'.

•
•

Stage 1: High Stationary (High Birth Rate, High Death Rate -> Stable Population): No Indian
state is in this stage now.
Stage 2: Early Expanding (High Birth Rate, Falling Death Rate -> Population Explosion): This
was India's situation in the mid-20th century.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

67

www.visionias.in

# 8468022022

©Vision IAS

•
•
•

Stage 3: Late Expanding (Falling Birth Rate, Low Death Rate -> Growth Slows): This is
where most of India is. However, large northern states like Bihar and Uttar Pradesh are
in the early part of this stage, with relatively high TFR and a large youth population.
Stage 4: Low Stationary (Low Birth Rate, Low Death Rate -> Stable Population): Southern
states like Kerala and Tamil Nadu have already entered or are close to entering this stage,
with below-replacement level TFR and an aging population.
Stage 5: Declining (Birth Rate falls below Death Rate -> Population Shrinks): This is the
stage of 'demographic winter' that some European countries and Japan are experiencing.

This divide means India has to formulate two sets of policies simultaneously: creating jobs
and providing education for the youth bulge in the North, while also building social security
and geriatric healthcare systems for the aging population in the South.

1.4. Understanding Population Structure
•

The Population Pyramid: This is a graphical representation of the age and sex composition
of a population. Its shape provides crucial insights into a country's demographic past and
future.
○ Expansive Pyramid: A broad base and narrow top, indicating high birth rates, high death
rates, and a young population (typical of Stage 2/3 of DTM).
○ Constrictive Pyramid: A narrow base, indicating low birth rates and an aging population
(typical of Stage 4/5).
○ Stationary Pyramid: A more rectangular shape, indicating low birth and death rates and
a stable population.

1.5. Factors of Population Growth in India
India's population has grown tremendously in recent decades, understanding the underlying
reasons is crucial. Here's a breakdown of the key factors:

•

Social Factors:
o High Fertility Rates:
> Traditionally, large families are valued, with religious beliefs often encouraging
childbirth.
> Lack of access to family planning services further contributes to higher birth rates.
o Low Literacy Rates: Particularly among women, lower literacy levels can correlate with
a lack of awareness about family planning options and reproductive health.
o Child Marriage: Child marriage, despite being outlawed, is still practiced in some parts
of India. This forces young girls to have children before their bodies are fully developed,
often leading to more pregnancies throughout their lives.
o Decreased Death Tolls: India has made tremendous strides in disease prevention,
sanitation, and healthcare throughout the years. Lower death rates have resulted from
these advancements, particularly for children and newborns. Naturally, the population
grows as more people live into adulthood
Cultural Factors:
o Son Preference: In some parts of India, a strong preference for sons for social or
inheritance reasons can lead to larger families until a son is born.
o Religious Beliefs: Certain religions may emphasize the importance of procreation,
influencing family size decisions.
Economic Factors:
o Poverty: In some cases, children are seen as additional laborers or future financial
security, especially in rural areas.
o Lack of Social Security: With limited social security systems, families might rely on
children for support in old age, fostering larger families.

2. The Demographic Dividend: India's Opportunity &
Challenge
2.1. Understanding India's Youth Bulge
•

•

•

Defining the Demographic Dividend:
○ The demographic dividend refers to the
potential for accelerated economic
growth that can result from a decline in
a country's birth and death rates and
the subsequent change in the age
structure of the population.
○ As fertility rates fall, the share of the
young,
dependent
population
decreases, while the share of the
working-age population (15-59 years)
increases. This leads to a lower
dependency ratio (fewer dependents
being supported by each workingperson) —freeing up resources for
investment and savings.
India's Window of Opportunity:
○ India has one of the youngest populations in the world, with a median age of around 28
years. Its working-age population is expected to constitute over 68% of the total
population by 2030.
○ According to the UNFPA, this demographic window for India is available from 2005-06
to 2055-56, longer than any other country in the world. This gives India a unique
advantage in the global economy.
The Two Dividends: The demographic dividend can be realized in two phases:
○ The First Dividend: This is generated by the demographic shift itself—the immediate
economic benefit of having a larger workforce and fewer dependents.
○ The Second Dividend (Silver Dividend): This is generated when the large working-age
population from the first phase begins to age and has accumulated significant savings. If
these savings are chanelled into productive investments, it can spur further economic
growth.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

70

www.visionias.in

# 8468022022

©Vision IAS

Student Notes:

2.2. The Challenge: Dividend or Disaster?

Student Notes:

The demographic dividend is not a guarantee; it is an opportunity that can be easily squandered.
If India fails to address key challenges, the dividend could transform into a demographic disaster.
•

The Challenge of "Jobless Growth":
○ While the economy has grown, millions of youth entering the workforce each year
struggle to find quality jobs.
○ This leads to high rates of unemployment and underemployment, especially among the
educated youth, causing widespread social frustration and potential instability.
The Crisis of Low Human Capital: The productivity and employability of the workforce
depend on its human capital, which remains a major concern in India.
○ Health & Nutrition: A workforce burdened by poor health cannot be productive. High
rates of malnutrition and anemia (57% in women) mean that many young people enter
the workforce with compromised physical and cognitive abilities.
○ Education & Learning Outcomes: Despite high enrollment rates, the quality of education
is poor. Reports like the ASER (Annual Status of Education Report) consistently show
low learning outcomes, with many children unable to do basic reading or arithmetic.
○ The Skills Gap: There is a significant mismatch between the skills of the available
workforce and the demands of the modern economy. Reports indicate that only a small
percentage of India's workforce has received formal vocational training, rendering many
"unemployable."

•

Case Study: The North-South Divide in India's Demographic Dividend
India's demographic dividend is not uniform across states, creating a stark North-South divide.
•

•

Southern States (e.g., Kerala, Tamil Nadu): These states have already experienced their
demographic transition. Their TFR is low, and their populations are aging faster. Their
demographic window is closing, and their policy challenge is now to manage an aging
workforce and build a robust social security system.
Northern States (e.g., Bihar, Uttar Pradesh): These states have a larger youth bulge and
their demographic window will last longer. Their challenge is more fundamental: to
improve basic health and education infrastructure and create jobs for their massive young
populations. This requires region-specific strategies.

The Human Capital Imperative: Realizing the Dividend
Converting India's youth bulge into a genuine demographic dividend requires strategic,
mission-mode investments in human capital. This rests on three essential pillars:
•

•

Health & Nutrition:
○ Focus: Tackling the twin challenges of childhood stunting and widespread anemia
among adolescents and women is non-negotiable. A healthy population is the
foundation of a productive workforce.
○ Example: Schemes like Poshan Abhiyan (National Nutrition Mission) and the MidDay Meal Scheme are critical interventions that need effective implementation to
build a physically and cognitively strong workforce from an early age.
Transforming Education:
○ Focus: The education system must shift from rote learning to developing critical
thinking, problem-solving, and adaptive skills. The goal is to improve learning
outcomes, not just enrollment rates.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

71

www.visionias.in

# 8468022022

©Vision IAS

○
•

Example: The National Education Policy (NEP) 2020 aims to achieve this by
introducing a more flexible curriculum, promoting multi-disciplinary learning, and
integrating vocational education from the school level.
Skilling and Apprenticeships:
○ Focus: Bridging the massive skills gap between what the industry needs and what the
education system produces. This requires a strong focus on vocational training,
apprenticeships, and lifelong learning.
○ Example: The National Skill Development Mission and initiatives like the Pradhan
Mantri Kaushal Vikas Yojana (PMKVY) are designed to provide industry-relevant skill
training to millions of youth, though their effectiveness and scale remain key
challenges.

Ultimately, realizing the dividend depends entirely on the state's capacity to transform its
young population into a healthy, educated, and skilled human resource.

3. Population and Key Socio-Economic Issues
3.1. The Poverty-Population Nexus
The relationship between poverty and population is not a simple one-way street but a complex,
vicious cycle. The debate is whether a growing population causes poverty or if poverty is the
main driver of population growth.

•

Argument 1: High Population •
Growth as a Cause of Poverty
○ Resource

This

○ Lower Per Capita Income: A
large population base means
the benefits of economic

Argument 2: Poverty as the Main Cause of
Population Growth
This is the more widely accepted sociological and
developmental perspective.
○ High Infant Mortality: In conditions of
poverty, where healthcare is poor and IMR is
high, families tend to have more children as
an "insurance" against the potential loss of a
child.
○ Children as Economic Assets: For poor
households, especially in rural and informal
economies, children are often seen as

While it is a vicious cycle, the stronger causal link flows from poverty to high population growth.
Therefore, the most effective long-term population stabilization strategy is not coercive control
but a direct attack on poverty and deprivation, especially by empowering women.
Case Study: The BIMARU States
The acronym BIMARU (Bihar, Madhya Pradesh, Rajasthan, Uttar Pradesh) was coined to refer
to states with poor demographic and health indicators. These states continue to have both the
highest poverty rates in the country (as per NITI Aayog's Multidimensional Poverty Index) and
the highest Total Fertility Rates (TFRs).
This provides a real-world illustration of the poverty-population vicious cycle, where socioeconomic backwardness and high fertility reinforce each other.

3.2. Human Development vs. Economic Growth
•

The Paradox: While India has been one of the world's fastest-growing major economies for
decades, its rank in the Human Development Index (HDI) remains low (134/191). This
indicates that the benefits of GDP growth have not translated into a commensurate
improvement in the quality of life for the average citizen.
Reasons for the Disconnect:
○ Unequal Distribution of Growth: Economic growth has been highly unequal. The rise of
a high-skill service economy has created immense wealth for some, but this has not been
accompanied by sufficient job creation for the masses, a phenomenon often called
"jobless growth."
○ Inadequate Public Spending: India's public expenditure on crucial sectors like health
(around 1.2% of GDP) and education (around 2.9% of GDP) is among the lowest in the
world, especially considering the needs of its vast population. This leads to poor quality
of public services.
○ Structural Inequalities: Deep-seated inequalities of caste, gender, and region prevent
marginalized groups from accessing the opportunities created by economic growth.

•

Eradicating Poverty by Liberating from Deprivation
The Core Idea (Amartya Sen's Capability Approach): This framework argues that development
should be assessed not just by income or GDP, but by the expansion of human capabilities—
the real freedoms and opportunities that people have to lead lives they value.
Poverty as Capability Deprivation:
From this perspective, poverty is not merely a lack of income but the deprivation of basic
capabilities. A person is poor if they lack the freedom from:
•

Illiteracy and Lack of Education

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

Student Notes:

Poor Health and Malnutrition
Lack of Political Voice and Social Exclusion
Lack of Personal Security

The statement "an essential condition to eradicate poverty is to liberate the poor from
deprivation" is thus validated.
•

•

Example 1 (Education): Simply giving a cash transfer to an illiterate person may provide
temporary relief. But providing them with education (liberating them from the deprivation
of illiteracy) gives them the capability to secure a better job, access information, and
escape poverty sustainably.
Example 2 (Health): Providing good public healthcare liberates the poor from the
deprivation of ill health and the crippling cost of private treatment, which is a major cause
of families falling back into poverty.

This approach argues for a focus on human development (health, education, agency) as the
most effective and sustainable way to eradicate poverty.

3.3. Population and Environmental Stress
A large and growing population exerts immense pressure on the natural environment.
•

Resource Depletion: A large population base intensifies the demand for natural resources,
leading to:
○ Land Degradation: Fragmentation of agricultural land holdings and conversion of forest
and farm land for settlement and infrastructure.
○ Water Scarcity: Over-extraction of groundwater for agriculture and domestic use,
leading to depleting water tables in many parts of India.
○ Deforestation: Pressure on forests for land and resources.
Pollution:
○ High population density, particularly in unplanned urban areas, leads to increased air,
water, and soil pollution.
○ The challenge of managing massive amounts of municipal solid waste generated by a
large urban population is a major environmental crisis.

•

Case Study: The Bengaluru Water Crisis (2024)
The recent severe water crisis in Bengaluru
is a stark example of the populationenvironment nexus. The city's explosive
population growth, fueled by the IT boom,
led to rapid and unplanned urbanization.
•

•

Impact: This resulted in the widespread
encroachment of the city's lakes and
water bodies, which were crucial for
rainwater storage and groundwater
recharge.
Consequence: With its natural water
systems destroyed and groundwater
levels depleted, the city faced an acute water shortage, forcing residents to rely on
expensive water tankers. This crisis highlights the severe environmental consequences of
supporting a large population without sustainable planning.

4. Gender, Aging, and Migration

Student Notes:

4.1. The Gendered Dimension of Population
Gender is not just a variable but a central axis that shapes and is shaped by population trends.
•

•

The 'Missing Women' Phenomenon and Skewed Sex Ratio:
○ This concept, highlighted by
economist Amartya Sen, refers
to the millions of women who
are "missing" from the
population
due
to
a
combination
of
neglect,
inadequate healthcare, and,
most significantly, sex-selective
abortion.
○ It is a direct consequence of
deep-seated son preference,
which is rooted in patriarchal
structures where sons inherit
property, carry on the family
name, and are seen as old-age security.
○ The Data: This bias is reflected in the adverse Sex Ratio at Birth (SRB), which, despite
improving to 929 girls per 1,000 boys (NFHS-5), remains well below the natural norm of
~952. The historical Child Sex Ratio (0-6 years) of 919 (Census 2011) highlights the
severity of this issue.
Women's Empowerment and Population Stabilization: There is a direct and established
correlation between women's empowerment and demographic outcomes. Empowering
women is the most effective "contraceptive" and the key to sustainable population
stabilization.
○ Education: Higher female education is strongly linked to a higher age of marriage, better
health awareness, greater participation in household decision-making, and lower fertility
rates. An educated woman is more likely to desire a smaller family and have access to
family planning methods.
○ Economic Agency: Financially independent women often have greater control over their
reproductive choices and invest more in the health and education of their children.
○ Health Access: Empowered women have better access to and control over contraception
and maternal healthcare services, leading to lower TFR and MMR.

The 'Beti Bachao, Beti Padhao' (BBBP) Scheme
Launched in 2015, the BBBP scheme is a flagship government program aimed at addressing
the declining Child Sex Ratio and promoting girls' education.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

75

www.visionias.in

# 8468022022

©Vision IAS

•
•

Approach: It uses mass media campaigns to challenge the mindset of son preference and
provides targeted interventions in districts with the most skewed sex ratios.
Impact: The scheme has been credited with an improvement in the Sex Ratio at Birth
(SRB) in many of the targeted districts. However, critics point out that changing deepseated patriarchal norms requires sustained, long-term societal effort beyond
government campaigns.

4.2. The Aging of India's Population
India is transitioning from being a young country to an aging society at a rapid pace.
•

The
Trend:
The
population of the
elderly (60+ years) is
currently around 10.5%
(~150 million) and is
projected to rise to
nearly 20% of the total
population by 2050. India's elderly population is growing faster than its overall population
growth rate.
Key Challenges of an Aging Society:
○ Economic Insecurity: A very small fraction of the elderly population is covered by formal
pension schemes. This leads to widespread economic dependency, especially as informal
sector workers have no retirement benefits.
○ Health Challenges: There is a need for robust geriatric healthcare to manage the rising
burden of chronic and non-communicable diseases (NCDs) like hypertension, diabetes,
and dementia.
○ Social & Emotional Issues: The breakdown of the traditional joint family system and the
migration of children have led to increasing social isolation, loneliness, and
psychological distress among the elderly.

•

Feminization of Aging: Women tend to live longer than men. Consequently, a large proportion
of the elderly population consists of widows, who often face compounded vulnerability due
to property disputes, financial dependency, and social neglect.
•
•
•
•

Caregiver Burden: Elderly women often take on the primary caregiver role for
grandchildren, children, and in-laws, adding to their already heavy burden. This can lead
to neglect of their own health and well-being.
Widowhood: A significant proportion of elderly women experience widowhood, leading
to social isolation, emotional distress, and increased vulnerability to abuse and neglect.
Property Rights: Despite legal provisions, many elderly women face challenges in
accessing property rights, leaving them economically and socially disadvantaged.
Neglect of Women’s Health: Women's health needs, particularly in old age, often receive
less attention compared to men. This includes inadequate access to preventive care,
chronic disease management, and geriatric care.

4.3. Migration Trends and Challenges
Migration is a central feature of India's population dynamics, reflecting both distress and
aspiration. The patterns of migration have transformed significantly over the last four decades.

Challenges Faced by Internal Migrants:
•

Precarious Labor: Many migrants face low wages and job insecurity. 60% of India's migrant
workforce is "occupationally vulnerable."
Limited Social Security: Most states lack mechanisms to support migrants, hindering access
to healthcare, education, and welfare. 70% of migrant children lack education facilities.
Inadequate Housing: Migrants often live in poor urban slums; 60% of slum dwellers are
migrants.
Disenfranchisement: Migrants face voting registration issues. 25% are registered to vote in
new locations.

Case Study: The "Surat Story" - A Hub for Internal Migration
Surat in Gujarat is a prime example of a city whose economy is built on the back of millions of
internal migrants.
•
•

The Pull: Its thriving textile and diamond industries have historically attracted a massive
workforce from states like Uttar Pradesh, Bihar, Odisha, and Maharashtra.
The Reality: This continuous flow of migration has fueled the city's economic engine.
However, it has also led to immense challenges in providing affordable housing, water,
sanitation, and healthcare for this large migrant population, which often lives in
precarious, overcrowded informal settlements. The city's resilience is frequently tested by
public health challenges linked to its high population density.

Internal Displacement: Uprooted Within
Internal displacement refers to the forced movement of people within their own country due
to conflict, violence, or natural disasters.
The Global Report on Internal Displacement (GRID) 2024 an estimated 75.9 million people were
internally displaced worldwide, reflecting a significant rise from 71.1 million in 2022.
India's Internal Displacement Landscape
While South Asia, including India, reported its lowest internal displacement figure (3.7 million
as per the GRID report) since 2018, the issue remains a pressing concern. This reduction can be
attributed to below-average monsoon rainfall and a weaker cyclone season due to El Nino's
influence. However, it's crucial to recognize the complexities within this data.
•

Forced From Home: Despite the decrease in disaster-related displacements, India
witnessed a concerning rise in internal displacement due to conflict within its borders.

The report highlights Manipur as a particular area of concern. In 2023 alone, an estimated
67,000 people were displaced by the conflict in this region, marking the highest number
since 2018. These displaced individuals remain uprooted within India, struggling to rebuild
their lives and facing immense hardship.
Climate Refugees: India's vast coastline makes it particularly vulnerable to cyclones and
rising sea levels, while extreme weather events like floods and droughts can disrupt
livelihoods and force people from their homes.
o The Road Ahead: Addressing Internal Displacement
o Addressing internal displacement requires a multi-pronged approach:
Conflict Resolution: Peaceful solutions to ongoing conflicts are crucial to prevent further
displacement and enable the safe return of displaced populations.
Disaster Risk Reduction: Investing in early warning systems, disaster preparedness
measures, and climate-resilient infrastructure can help minimize displacement caused by
natural disasters.
Support for the Displaced: Providing humanitarian assistance to meet the immediate needs
of displaced populations, along with long-term solutions like livelihood opportunities and
access to education, is essential for their reintegration and rebuilding their lives.

Challenges faced by International Migrants
•

•
•

•

Wage Exploitation and Unfair Labor Practices: migrants in low-skilled sectors, face
exploitation by recruitment agencies and employers. A 2023 report by Human Rights Watch
documented cases of wage theft, passport confiscation, and hazardous working conditions
faced by Indian migrant workers in the Gulf countries.
○ In 2022, Indian construction workers in Qatar protested against unpaid wages and poor
working conditions, tied to the 2022 FIFA World Cup infrastructure.
Social and Cultural Integration: Adapting to a new culture, language, and social environment
can leads to feelings of isolation and loneliness and health concerns.
Separation from Families: Leaving behind family and loved ones is an emotional struggle for
many migrants. Technological advancements like video calls and messaging apps can help
bridge the physical distance, but the emotional strain of separation remains a significant
challenge.
Challenges on Return: Returning migrants, especially those who spent a significant time
abroad, may face difficulties re-integrating into the Indian job market. Their skills and
qualifications might not be readily recognized, leading to unemployment and
underemployment.

Since migration has cross-cutting sectoral impacts, multiple and complementary interventions
by different ministries and departments are needed, to facilitate migration and ensure
integration of migrants into the economic, social, political and cultural life of the country.

4.4. Social Capital
Social capital refers to the benefits people gain from their networks of relationships. It's
essentially the value of those connections. Here's a breakdown of the concept and its
significance:
What it is:
•
•
•

Networks and Relationships: Social capital is built on the connections we have with others,
including family, friends, colleagues, neighbors, and even online communities.
Trust and Reciprocity: These connections are strengthened by trust and reciprocity, meaning
we can rely on each other and exchange favors or support.
Shared Values and Norms: Strong social capital often involves shared values and norms that
guide behavior within the network.

Why it's important:
•
•
•

Individual Benefits: Strong social capital provides individuals with access to resources,
information, and opportunities. It can lead to better jobs, educational attainment, and even
improved health outcomes.
Collective Benefits: Strong social capital benefits society as a whole. It fosters cooperation,
reduces crime rates, and strengthens social institutions. It can also lead to more effective
problem-solving and innovation.
Economic Growth: Social capital is seen as a key factor in economic development. It
facilitates trust-based transactions, reduces information asymmetry, and encourages
entrepreneurship
○ Social capital can facilitate knowledge sharing and access to opportunities, especially for
disadvantaged groups. This can help young people from lower socio-economic
backgrounds acquire the skills and education needed to contribute effectively to the
workforce.

Challenges to the Social capital development in India:
•

Decay: Traditional social structures like extended families and close-knit neighbourhoods
are weakening. People become more mobile, and social interactions can become more

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

81

www.visionias.in

# 8468022022

©Vision IAS

•
•

fleeting. Loss of trust, weakened support systems, and decreased sense of belonging can
result.
Lack of Equity: Unequal distribution of social capital. Marginalized groups might lack access
to powerful networks or resources that benefit more privileged groups. Perpetuates social
inequalities and limits opportunities for those who need them most.
Separatism: Social divisions based on factors like religion, ethnicity, or ideology can create
strong "in-groups" that exclude others. This hinders collaboration and trust across
communities
o Example: Strong political polarization can lead to social divisions, where people only
interact with those who share their views, limiting exposure to diverse perspectives.

Here are some ways to improve and develop India's social capital:
•

•

•

Strengthening Social Networks and Trust:
o Community-based initiatives: Encourage and support resident welfare associations,
self-help groups, and local social clubs. These can foster connections and build a sense
of belonging.
o Civic engagement projects: Promote volunteerism and community service
opportunities. Working together on local issues can build trust and collaboration.
o Interfaith and intercultural dialogues: Encourage understanding and respect between
different religious and cultural groups. This can break down social barriers and build
bridges.
Investing in Social Institutions:
o Strengthening local governance: Empower local government bodies to be more
responsive to citizen needs. This can increase trust in institutions and encourage
participation.
o Media literacy education: Equip citizens with the skills to critically evaluate information
and engage in constructive dialogue. This can combat misinformation and promote a
more informed society.
Promoting Social Mobility and Inclusion:
o Education for all: Invest in quality education, particularly for girls and marginalized
communities. Education empowers individuals and fosters social mobility.
o Diversity and inclusion initiatives: Encourage workplaces and public institutions to be
inclusive of different backgrounds and perspectives. This can create a more cohesive
society.
o Bridging the digital divide: Ensure equitable access to technology and the internet. This
can connect people and empower them to participate in the digital economy.

Overall, strong social capital can act as a multiplier for India's demographic dividend. By fostering
trust, cooperation, and opportunity, it can empower young people, create a more enabling
environment for businesses, and ultimately lead to more inclusive and sustainable economic
growth.

5. Population Policies in India
India, now as the most populous country in the world, has implemented several population
policies aimed at controlling the growth rate and improving the quality of life of its citizens. These
policies have evolved over time, reflecting changing socio-economic conditions and priorities.

5.1. National Population Policy 2000
The National Population Policy (NPP) 2000 was a significant milestone in India's efforts to
stabilize its population.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

Student Notes:

Immediate objectives
•
•

Address unmet needs for contraception, healthcare infrastructure, and healthcare
personnel.
Provide integrated service delivery for basic reproductive and child healthcare.

Medium-Term Objectives:
•
•

Reduce the Total Fertility Rate (TFR) to replacement level (around 2.1 children per woman)
by 2010. This was intended to achieve population stabilization in the long run.
Above objectives will be achieve by working together across different government
departments and agencies

Long-Term Objectives:
•

Achieve a stable population by 2045, at a level consistent with sustainable economic growth,
social development, and environmental protection.

Key Policy Features:
•
•
•
•

Voluntary and informed choice: The policy emphasized the importance of voluntary family
planning and ensuring informed consent for services like sterilization.
Focus on women's empowerment: Education and improved access to healthcare for women
were seen as crucial factors in reducing fertility rates.
Improved service delivery: Upgrading infrastructure, increasing healthcare personnel, and
expanding access to family planning services across rural and urban areas were prioritized.
Integration with other sectors: The policy advocated collaboration with departments like
education and social welfare to address underlying factors like poverty and gender inequality
that contribute to population growth.

The NPP shifted the focus from solely reducing birth rates to a more holistic approach that
emphasized women's empowerment, improved healthcare, and responsible parenthood.

Revamping India's Population Policy: Need for a new population policy

India's existing population policy, the NPP 2000, needs an update. While it aimed for family
planning and lower maternal mortality, demographic realities have shifted.
•

A Looming Demographic Shift: India's young population presents a limited opportunity
for growth (peaking by 2041). However, a growing population strains resources. Fertility
decline, meanwhile, leads to a rapidly aging population (12% by 2025).

A revised policy should address these changes by:
•
•
•

Investing in Youth: Skilling and employing the youth to maximize the demographic
dividend.
Supporting the Elderly: Creating social and economic support systems for the growing
elderly population, with a focus on women due to their longer lifespans.
Focus on BIMARU States: Allocating more resources for education, skilling, and job
creation in states with higher fertility rates (Bihar, Madhya Pradesh, Rajasthan, and Uttar
Pradesh).

The focus should shift from mere population control to:
•
•

Sustainable Development: Encouraging smaller families through economic growth and
development.
Education and Skills: Equipping the workforce for the evolving job market impacted by
automation.

Two Child norms for population control
Several states, including Assam, Maharashtra, Madhya Pradesh, and Rajasthan, have
introduced a two-child norm prohibiting individuals with more than two children from
obtaining government jobs.
•

The rationale behind this policy is to control rapid population growth by limiting fertility
rates. By denying social and economic benefits, the policy aims to encourage prospective
parents to limit their number of children.

China's One-Child Policy: A Cautionary Tale.
Implemented from 1979 to 2015, it aimed to curb rapid population growth. While initially
lauded for its success in slowing population growth, the policy also had several unintended
and detrimental impacts:
•

•

•

Demographic Imbalance: created a significant gender imbalance, with millions more
men than women, affecting marriage and social stability.It led to a rapidly aging
population, straining social security and healthcare systems due to fewer working-age
adults supporting the elderly
Socioeconomic Issues: Labor shortages, caused by a shrinking workforce, hinder
economic growth.
o "Little Emperor" syndrome, resulting from single-child families, leads to
indulgence, fostering selfishness and entitlement among some younger
generations.
Ethical Concerns: The policy of forced sterilization and abortions, implemented with
varying strictness, raised ethical concerns and potentially had severe psychological
impacts on parents desiring larger families, highlighting significant human rights and
emotional issues.

5.2. Way Forward for Population Control
•

Focus on Demographic Dividend: India is at a pivotal juncture where its demographic
advantage can drive economic growth. Policymakers should focus on harnessing this
demographic dividend to fuel economic progress rather than viewing it as a challenge.
Focusing on Women: Women's education significantly impacts fertility rates and the age at
which they have their first child. Therefore, investing in women's education is vital for India's
overall development

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

85

www.visionias.in

# 8468022022

©Vision IAS

•

Empowering Informed Decisions: Policies should prioritize empowering both men and
women to make informed decisions regarding their fertility, health, and well-being.
o For example, comprehensive sex education in schools can provide young people with the
knowledge they need to make responsible choices about their sexual and reproductive
health.
o Additionally, access to healthcare services and family planning resources should be
expanded to support individuals in making educated decisions.
Caring for an Ageing Population: Developing institutional and state capacity is essential to
care for an ageing population, alongside creating an improved system for protecting the
elderly.
o For example, establishing more senior care centers and providing training for geriatric
care professionals can ensure that the ageing population receives adequate support.
o Additionally, implementing social security schemes and pension plans can provide
financial stability and security for the elderly, enhancing their quality of life.’

•

Apart from achieving the basic demographic goals, future policies must also address following
factors:
•

•

•
•

Address Skewed Sex Ratios: A future population policy must tackle the skewed female
and child sex ratios, particularly spreading from urban to rural areas, by combating
discriminatory practices like the lack of women’s ownership rights over land and property,
which perpetuate son preference.
Regulate Migration: The policy should address the challenges of interstate and intrastate
migration highlighted by Census 2011, such as unplanned migration to cities, which strains
infrastructure and creates social tensions. Planned migration policies can prevent the
formation of slums and unplanned habitations.
Promote Education and Skill Development: Resources must be balanced between
addressing the needs of the aging population and investing in education and skill
development to ensure a productive workforce for the future.
Long-term Planning: A comprehensive population policy that anticipates future
challenges and demographic shifts will protect future generations from catastrophic
outcomes by ensuring sustainable development and social stability.

Despite historic challenges due to male child preference and rural ignorance, India's family
planning efforts have reduced the growth rate from 2.20% in 1971 to 1.64% in 2011. The NFHSV indicates a fertility rate drop to 2.0, showing potential for further population control.

CHAPTER 4: POVERTY AND ITS IMPLICATIONS

Student Notes:

Key Data & Facts at a Glance
•

•

•

•

•

Multidimensional Poverty (NITI Aayog, 2023 Report):
○ Poverty Rate: India's multidimensional poverty has declined sharply from 29.17% in
2013-14 to 11.28% in 2022-23.
○ People Escaping Poverty: A total of 24.82 crore people moved out of multidimensional
poverty in the last nine years.
○ Rural vs. Urban: Rural poverty declined faster (from 32.59% to 19.28%) than urban
poverty (from 8.65% to 5.27%).
○ State-wise Disparity: Bihar, Jharkhand, and Meghalaya remain the poorest states, while
Kerala has the lowest poverty rate.
Income and Wealth Inequality:
○ Top vs. Bottom: According to the World Inequality Report 2022, the top 10% of the
Indian population holds 57% of the total national income, while the bottom 50% share
has gone down to 13%.
○ Wealth Concentration: An Oxfam Report (2023) stated that India's richest 1% own more
than 40% of the country's total wealth. This highlights the issue of "jobless growth,"
where economic growth does not translate into equitable wealth distribution.
Deprivation Indicators (NFHS-5, 2019-21):
○ Nutrition: 35.5% of children under 5 are stunted (low height-for-age).
○ Anemia: 57% of women and 67% of children are anemic.
○ Access to Basic Amenities: While access to improved sanitation (70%) and clean cooking
fuel (76%) has increased, significant gaps remain.
Poverty among Social Groups:
○ Caste & Tribe: Poverty rates are disproportionately high among marginalized groups.
The multidimensional poverty ratio is highest for STs (50.6%), followed by SCs (33.3%)
and OBCs (27.2%), compared to 'Others' (15.6%).
Global Context:
○ Extreme Poverty: According to the World Bank, the number of people living in extreme
poverty in India (less than $2.15 per day) declined to 12.9% in 2021.

Glossary of Essential Concepts
•

•

•
•

Poverty vs. Deprivation: A crucial distinction. Poverty is often measured by income or
consumption levels (e.g., the poverty line). Deprivation is a broader, multidimensional
concept referring to the lack of essential capabilities and freedoms, such as the freedom
from illiteracy, poor health, and social exclusion.
Absolute vs. Relative Poverty:
○ Absolute Poverty: A condition where household income is below a level needed to
maintain basic living standards (food, shelter, housing). This is what the poverty line
attempts to measure.
○ Relative Poverty: A condition where people lack the minimum amount of income
needed to maintain the average standard of living in the society in which they live. It is a
measure of inequality.
Poverty Line: A monetary threshold below which an individual is considered to be poor. It is
calculated based on the expenditure required to purchase a "poverty line basket" of
essential goods and services.
Multidimensional Poverty Index (MPI): A measure that captures acute poverty by assessing
deprivations across three key dimensions: Health (nutrition, child mortality), Education
(years of schooling, school attendance), and Living Standards (assets, housing, sanitation,
etc.). It provides a more holistic view than income-based measures.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

87

www.visionias.in

# 8468022022

©Vision IAS

•
•
•
•
•

Vicious Circle of Poverty: The theory that a country or individual is poor because they are
poor. It posits a circular constellation of forces where low income leads to low savings, which
leads to low investment, leading to low productivity, and thus back to low income.
Social Exclusion: The process through which individuals or groups are systematically blocked
from (or denied full access to) various rights, opportunities, and resources that are normally
available to members of a different group, and which are fundamental to social integration.
Intergenerational Poverty: The phenomenon of poverty being transmitted from one
generation to the next, creating a self-perpetuating cycle.
Feminization of Poverty: The trend that women represent a disproportionate percentage of
the world's poor. This is linked to the gender pay gap, limited access to resources, and the
burden of unpaid care work.
Poverty Trap: A self-reinforcing mechanism that causes poverty to persist. Any small amount
of extra income is likely to be spent on immediate consumption rather than being saved or
invested, thus trapping the individual in poverty.

1. What is Poverty?
The approach to defining poverty has usually been in economic term – the levels of income,
property and living standards. People are said to be poor when their income is such that it does
not enable them to meet the basic needs such as food, shelter, and clothing.
Beyond Income: A Multidimensional View
Modern perspectives recognize poverty as a complex phenomenon with sociological, political,
psychological, and geographical dimensions. This broader perspective acknowledges that income
is just one piece of the puzzle. Poverty can also be understood as the inability to develop one's
full potential due to limited access to education, healthcare, and social networks.
Measuring Poverty: Different Approaches
To assess poverty's extent and design effective interventions, various methodologies have
emerged:
•

•

Absolute Poverty: This approach defines a minimum income level (poverty line) necessary
to meet basic necessities. Individuals or households falling below this line are considered
absolutely poor. Here, factors like calorie intake, housing quality, and access to healthcare
are used as indicators.
Relative Poverty: This approach considers poverty relative to the prevailing living standards
within a society. Poverty lines are not fixed but shift based on societal norms and
expectations. For example, someone considered poor in the United States with its higher
standards of living might not be classified as such in India.

2. Poverty Line
The poverty line is a critical threshold that determines the minimum level of income,
consumption, or access to goods and services required for individuals to be considered non-poor.
•
•

This concept involves calculating the total expenditure needed for an adequate diet, clothing,
and shelter.
In India, it focuses on the cost of food to meet minimum calorie requirements.

Globally, the World Bank sets an International Poverty Line (IPL), updated to $1.90 per day in
2015.
Next, we delve into the evolution of poverty line estimation in India, detailing the methodologies
and expert groups involved post-independence.

2.1. Evolution of Poverty Estimation in India

Student Notes:

The methodology for estimating poverty in India has undergone significant changes over the
years.
•

Lakdawala Expert Group (1993): This group addressed regional variations by recommending
state-specific poverty lines based on different Consumer Price Indices (CPI) for industrial and
agricultural workers.
Tendulkar Expert Group (2009): This group aimed to modernize the methodology by:
○ Shifting from calorie-based norms to focus on broader nutritional outcomes.
○ Implementing a uniform all-India poverty line.
○ Including private spending on health and education.
Rangarajan Committee (2014): This committee proposed further revisions, suggesting
separate rural and urban poverty line baskets with specific food and non-food items.
NITI Aayog Task Force (2015): This group acknowledged ongoing debates and
recommended:
○ Tracking the progress of the bottom 30% of the population, not just those below the
poverty line.
○ Monitoring specific material poverty components like nutrition, housing, and
connectivity.

•

•
•

Challenges in Establishing a Poverty Line:
•
•

Cost of Basic Needs: Determining the exact cost of a basket of basic goods and services
(food, clothing, shelter) that defines the poverty line is a complex task. Factors like regional
variations, inflation, and changing consumption patterns make it a moving target.
Calorie Intake vs. Nutritional Outcomes: Previous poverty lines in India might have focused
primarily on minimum calorie intake. However, modern approaches consider broader
nutritional needs to ensure a healthy standard of living.

These developments reflect India's on-going effort to refine its poverty measurement strategy.

The Multidimensional Poverty Index (MPI) by NITI Aayog suggests that 14.96% of India's

Aayog report using the MPI, released in 2023.

Issues and the Way Forward
Despite its advantages, the NMPI faces some challenges:
•
•
•

Sensitivity: The requirement to be deprived in at least one-third of indicators makes the
NMPI less sensitive to minor deprivations.
COVID-19 Impact: Critics argue that the NMPI might not fully capture the impact of COVID19 on livelihoods and healthcare access.
Stagnant Wages: Concerns exist regarding the consistency of declining poverty rates with
stagnant real wages.

Moving Forward:
To address these issues, several recommendations have been proposed:
•
•
•

Include New Dimensions: Expanding the MPI to consider learning outcomes, social
discrimination, and environmental factors.
New Measurement: Incorporating data from the latest National Sample Survey (NSS)
round and National Family Health Survey (NFHS) into the MPI calculations.
Effective Monitoring: Monitoring progress across indicators on a real-time basis for a more
accurate picture.

Additionally, social audits of poverty alleviation programs and awareness generation about
development initiatives remain crucial in tackling multidimensional poverty in India.

(BPL) censuses. Notably, the latest comprehensive data comes from the Socio-Economic Caste
Census (SECC) conducted in 2011. The significance of SECC lies in:

3. Causes of Poverty
3.1. Nexus Between Inequality and Poverty
Inequality and poverty are intricately linked, reinforcing each other in a vicious cycle.
•

Unequal Distribution of Resources: A significant wealth gap exists in India. The richest 1%
own over a quarter of the national wealth, while a large portion of the population struggles
to meet basic needs. This skewed distribution restricts opportunities for the poor to climb
the economic ladder
Limited Social Safety Nets: A 2021 World Bank report states that only 25% of India's
population benefits from some form of social security. This lack of support leaves them
exposed to economic shocks and hinders their ability to escape poverty.

•

Manifestation and intersection of these inequalities:
Ruralization of Poverty:
•

•

Causes: Limited access to quality education, healthcare, and infrastructure restricts
income generation opportunities in rural areas. Dependence on agriculture makes
livelihoods vulnerable to factors like weather and volatile market prices. Land ownership
skewed towards men further disadvantages women.
Impact: Rural poverty disproportionately affects marginalized communities like tribals and
lower castes who are often concentrated in rural areas. Limited resources and
opportunities hinder their social mobility and perpetuate the cycle of poverty.

Regional Variations:
•
•
•

Poverty levels vary significantly across Indian states. According to the Tendulkar
Committee Report (2011), states like Bihar, Jharkhand, and Chhattisgarh have higher
poverty rates, while Kerala and Goa have lower rates.
Causes: Historical disadvantages, lack of investment in infrastructure and development in
certain regions, and geographical constraints like recurring droughts or floods contribute
to regional disparities.
Impact: Regional variations create an uneven playing field for people's economic
prospects. It necessitates targeted policies and resource allocation to bridge the
development gap between states.

Caste and Religion Intersection:
•
•

The National Socioeconomic Survey (2011) shows significantly higher poverty rates among
Scheduled Castes (SCs) and Scheduled Tribes (STs) compared to upper castes. Similarly,
religious minorities may face social and economic discrimination.
Causes: The caste system historically relegated SCs and STs to menial jobs with low wages.
Societal prejudice and limited access to education and resources perpetuate their socioeconomic marginalization. Religious minorities may face discrimination in employment
and land ownership.

Feminization of Poverty:
•

The 'feminization of poverty' refers to the increasing proportion of women among the
poor. Systemic gender inequalities limit women's access to resources, opportunities, and
rights, making them more vulnerable to economic hardship.

Key Indicators:
•
•
•
•
•
•

Wage Gap: Women earn approximately Rs 40 for every Rs 100 earned by men.
Labour Force Participation: Only 41.7 % of women are part of the labor force.
GDP Contribution: Women contribute just 17% to the GDP.
Unpaid Care Work: Women spend significantly more time on unpaid care work
compared to men.
Education Disparities: Girls often face limited access to education, perpetuating the
cycle of poverty.
Impact on Women: The feminization of poverty significantly impacts women in various
ways, including economic vulnerability, educational disadvantages, limited time for paid
employment, and increased health and safety risks. Social norms and violence further
hinder their advancement.

3.2. Vicious Circle Theory
This theory argues that the poor are trapped in circumstances which make it difficult for them to
escape poverty.
•

•

•

Low Levels of Education: Poverty often leads to limited access to quality education. Children
from poor families may be forced to work or attend under-resourced schools with a studentteacher ratio of 60:1 (Source: UNESCO Institute for Statistics data, 2021), hindering their
ability to acquire skills needed for better jobs.
Poor Health and Malnutrition: A 2019 study by the Centre for Chronic Poverty Studies found
that chronic malnutrition among children in tribal communities of Madhya Pradesh is linked
to low school enrollment and limited future opportunities, highlighting the intergenerational
impact of poverty and malnutrition.
High Fertility Rates: A 2021 report by the Population Reference Bureau indicated that India's
Total Fertility Rate (TFR) is 2.0, but remains higher in some states with higher poverty levels.
This can make it challenging for families to invest in each child's education and well-being,
potentially leading to the continuation of poverty across generations.

3.3. Geographical Factors
Poverty is explained at times by the geographical conditions in which people live. The resources
are scarce and people are unable to overcome the problems of lack of resources. The desert
areas, the hill areas are good examples.
•
•
•

Regional Disparity: Poverty is not evenly distributed across India. According to the 2011
Socio-Economic Caste Census (SECC) data, Bihar has a poverty rate of 51.9%, while Goa has
a rate of 5.1%.
Natural Disasters: India is vulnerable to natural disasters like floods, droughts, and cyclones.
A 2020 World Bank report estimates that natural disasters push an additional 2.4 million
people into poverty in India each year.
A 2022 study by the International Food Policy Research Institute (IFPRI) suggests that climate
change could push an additional 36 million Indians into poverty by 2030.

4. Consequences of Poverty
Poverty is a complex issue that extends far beyond the lack of material resources. It has a
profound impact on individuals, families, and society as a whole, creating a vicious cycle that can
be difficult to escape. Here's a closer look at some of its key social consequences:
•

Culture of Poverty:
The concept of a "culture of poverty" argues that poverty can become ingrained in
