communities across generations. Factors like limited access to education, unemployment,
and inadequate housing can lead to feelings of hopelessness and a sense of powerlessness.
This, in turn, can discourage individuals from taking initiatives to improve their
circumstances, perpetuating the cycle.
•

Effects on Health:
Poverty has a direct and undeniable impact on health. Poor living conditions, malnutrition,
and lack of access to quality healthcare contribute to higher infant mortality rates, increased
vulnerability to diseases, and a higher prevalence of mental health issues.
○

•

A 2020 World Health Organization report found that people in the lowest income quintile
live an average of 18 fewer years than those in the highest.

Stolen Childhoods: Limited Education, Child Labor, and Early Marriage
Children are especially vulnerable to the consequences of poverty. A 2021 UNICEF report
estimates that 606 million children globally live in extreme poverty. This translates to a web
of challenges:
○

○
○

•

Limited Education: Poor infrastructure, unemployment, and lack of basic services lead
to inadequate schooling, hindering children's academic achievement and future job
prospects.
Child Labor: The harsh realities of poverty force many children to work, sacrificing
education and childhood for the family's survival.
Early Marriage: For girls, poverty can be a driver of child marriage, limiting their
autonomy, hindering education, and posing health risks. Studies indicate that poor girls
are 2.5 times more likely to marry young compared to wealthier backgrounds.

Psychological Effects: The constant struggle to meet basic needs can take a toll on mental

5. Schemes and policies to alleviate poverty in India
India has implemented various strategies to alleviate poverty. These efforts have evolved over
the decades, reflecting changes in economic policies, development priorities, and governance
approaches.

The Indian government tackles poverty through a comprehensive range of programs, aiming to
empower individuals and families to break the cycle of economic hardship.
Livelihood Generation:
•
•
•

Integrated Rural Development Programme (IRDP) (1978): Provides subsidies and bank loans
to create sustainable income opportunities in rural areas.
National Rural Livelihood Mission (Aajeevika) (2011): Focuses on skill development and
Self-Help Groups (SHGs) to diversify income sources for rural families.
National Urban Livelihood Mission (NULM) (2013): Empowers the urban poor by promoting
SHGs, skill training, and access to credit for self-employment ventures.

Food Security and Employment:
•
•
•

Food for Work Programme (2000): Provides food grains as wages for rural employment
projects, ensuring food security while creating work opportunities.
Mahatma Gandhi National Rural Employment Guarantee Act (MGNREGA) (2005):
Guarantees 100 days of paid work annually in rural areas, particularly for women.
National Food Security Mission (2007): Aims to boost agricultural production, create farm
jobs, and improve farm income.

Social Security and Financial Inclusion:
•
•

National Social Assistance Programme (NSAP): Offers various social security benefits such
as old age pensions and maternity benefits to the most disadvantaged.
Pradhan Mantri Jan Dhan Yojana: Facilitates financial inclusion by opening bank accounts
for the unbanked poor, enabling direct transfer of subsidies and benefits.

India's fight against poverty requires a multi-pronged approach. Here's how the country can
tackle problem of poverty

Invest in Social Sectors: Empowering the Underprivileged
•

Increase access to quality education, particularly for girls. Example: Expanding the "Beti
Bachao Beti Padhao" (Save the Girl Child, Educate the Girl Child) campaign to address gender
disparity in education. According to a World Bank report (World Bank, World Development
Report 2018, for every additional year of schooling, a person's earnings increase by an

Increase investment in sanitation infrastructure and hygiene awareness campaigns. Scaling
up initiatives like the Swachh Bharat Abhiyan (Clean India Mission) to improve sanitation
coverage in rural areas.

•

Foster Inclusive Growth: Creating Opportunities for All
•

Focus on skill development programs aligned with industry needs. Example: Expanding

•

Implement policies that promote fair wages and address income inequality.Example:

Build Climate Resilience: Mitigating the Impact of Climate Change
•

Invest in climate-smart agriculture practices and infrastructure.
○ Example: Promoting drought-resistant crop varieties and efficient irrigation systems to
help farmers adapt to changing weather patterns.

Strengthen Social Safety Nets: Protecting the Vulnerable
•

Expand and improve social safety net programs like the Public Distribution System (PDS) to
ensure food security for vulnerable populations. Utilizing technology and improving PDS
targeting to ensure benefits reach those who need them most.
UBI as a solution
Universal Basic Income (UBI) is a financial policy where the government provides all citizens
with a regular, unconditional sum of money, regardless of their income, employment status,
or wealth. The primary goal of UBI is to ensure that every citizen has enough income to meet
their basic needs.
Arguments for UBI:
•

•

•

Income Security: UBI guarantees a minimum income level for all citizens, providing a
safety net that ensures no one falls below a certain standard of living.
○ India - SEWA Pilot Project (2011-2013): In Madhya Pradesh, villagers received a
monthly basic income. The results showed improved nutrition, health, school
attendance, and economic activity.
Empowerment: UBI can empower individuals by granting them greater control over their
finances. This can lead to improved life choices, such as pursuing education or starting
small businesses.
○ UBI can stimulate local economies by increasing purchasing power. In the Kenyan
experiment, participants invested in livestock, farming, and small businesses, boosting
economic activity in their communities.
Simplification of Welfare Systems: UBI can replace complex welfare schemes, reducing
administrative costs and ensuring that aid reaches that in need without bureaucratic
hurdles.

Arguments Against UBI:
•

Fiscal Sustainability: The primary concern surrounding UBI in India is its affordability.
Providing a basic income to all citizens would require a significant financial outlay. Critics

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

96

www.visionias.in

# 8468022022

©Vision IAS

•

•

argue that these funds could be better directed towards targeted poverty alleviation
programs with proven track records.
Inflation and Disincentive to Work: A universal cash transfer could lead to inflation,
especially in the short term. Additionally, some argue that a guaranteed income might
discourage people from seeking employment, potentially impacting the labor market.
○ Finland's UBI experiment with 2,000 unemployed individuals receiving €560 per
month showed improved mental health and well-being. The study highlighted the
potential of UBI to simplify welfare systems and improve quality of life without
discouraging work.
Targeting and Efficiency: With limited resources, some argue that UBI might not be the
most efficient way to address poverty. Targeted programs might be more effective in
reaching the most vulnerable sections of society. Additionally, concerns exist about how
UBI would be adjusted for regional variations in living costs.

While UBI offers a potential solution, it shouldn't be seen as a standalone strategy.
Empowering the underprivileged, fostering inclusive growth, building climate resilience, and
strengthening social safety nets are crucial steps towards alleviating poverty and ensuring a
brighter future for all Indians.

% Urbanization in India: India’s population stood at 1210 million in 2011, with an

receive safe tap water, and most rely on filtration.
Rural-Urban Divide: 70% of India’s population lives in rural areas but contributes only 25%
to GDP (NITI Aayog, 2021).

Glossary of Essential Concepts
•
•
•
•
•

Urbanization: It is the increase in the proportion of people living in towns and cities.
Urbanism: It is the way of living or characteristics of lifestyle of people living in urban areas.
Ghetto: A ghetto refers to a neighbour-hood primarily segregated along ethnic, racial,
communal, or caste lines, where a particular group, often marginalized, is relegated due to
discrimination or social exclusion.
Enclave: An enclave is a self-segregated area where a particular ethnic, communal, or social
group voluntarily chooses to live separately, often to preserve their cultural identity or for
social cohesion. It is a space of intentional separation rather than forced isolation.
Slum: A slum denotes a neighbour-hood characterized by economic or class-based
segregation, where people, often from lower-income backgrounds, are forced to live in
substandard conditions due to poverty.

Citadel: A citadel is a neighbour-hood or area where affluent or wealthy individuals isolate
themselves from the rest of society, typically to maintain a higher standard of living, security,
and exclusivity.
Marginalization: Exclusion of low-income groups from urban services and decision-making.
Peri-urban areas: Transitional zones between fully urban and rural areas, exhibiting mixed
land use and characteristics
Urban Sprawl: Unplanned expansion of cities into peripheral areas.

1. Understanding Urbanization and Urban Life
1.1. Defining 'Urban' and 'Urbanization'
Urbanization refers to the process of an increasing proportion of a country's population living
in towns and cities. This demographic shift is a global phenomenon, but its definition and
manifestations vary.
An 'urban' area is typically characterized by higher population density, predominance of nonagricultural employment, and specific administrative or infrastructural markers.
The Indian Definition: India employs a two-pronged approach to classify urban centers:
•
•

Statutory Towns: Areas administered by a civic body like a municipal corporation,
municipality, cantonment board, or notified town area committee.
Census Towns: Settlements fulfilling all three criteria:
○ Minimum population of 5,000.
○ At least 75% of the male main working population engaged in non-agricultural activities.
○ Population density of at least 400 persons per sq. km.

The number of Census Towns in India surged dramatically from 1,362 in 2001 to 3,894 in 2011.
This highlights widespread 'hidden urbanization' – areas acquiring urban characteristics without
formal urban status.
•

Example – Kerala's Urban Transition: Kerala exhibits a unique pattern where numerous
large, dense panchayats meet Census Town criteria, reflecting urbanization extending
beyond traditionally defined municipal limits. This often results in a lag in providing urbanlevel amenities and governance to these functionally urban areas.

The Hidden Urbanisation in India & Its Implications
India's official definition of 'urban' has been termed "restrictive" or "reluctant." The strict
three-fold criteria for Census Towns (especially the 75% male non-agricultural workforce)
often exclude many large, dense settlements that function like towns but remain classified as
'rural'. This has significant implications:
•
•

•

Underestimation: The actual urban population and pace of urbanization might be higher.
The World Bank’s ‘Agglomeration Index’ indicated 55.3% of Indians (2010) lived in urbanlike settlements, which is far above official figures.
Planning Deficits: Settlements that are 'urban in character' but 'rural in classification'
often miss out on urban development schemes, infrastructure (like piped water,
sanitation, planned roads), and empowered local governance, leading to a poor quality of
life.
'Subaltern Urbanism': This term refers to urbanization processes driven by the poor and
marginalized, often occurring in these overlooked, informally urbanizing spaces,
highlighting a gap between official recognition and ground reality.

1.2. Urbanization Trends and the Nature of Indian Urban
Experience
Globally, urbanization is a defining trend of the 21st century. In India, while the level of
urbanization (31.16% in 2011) is moderate compared to China's ~57% in 2023, the absolute
number of urban dwellers (377.1 million in 2011) is immense. The UN projects India's urban
population to reach 877 million by 2050.

1.3. 'Urbanism' as a Way of Life: Social and Psychological
Dimensions
Urbanism refers to the distinct social and psychological characteristics of life in urban areas,
shaped by factors like population size, density, and heterogeneity.
•

Key Features of Urban Social Life:
○ Heterogeneity: Cities are melting pots of diverse ethnicities, cultures, religions, and
classes, leading to vibrant but complex social interactions.
○ Anonymity and Impersonal Relations: The scale of cities fosters anonymity, offering
freedom from traditional social controls but also leading to more formal, segmented,
and sometimes impersonal (secondary) relationships. For instance, daily interactions
with shopkeepers or fellow commuters are often functional rather than deeply personal.
○ Individualism and Specialization: Urban life often emphasizes individual achievement,
and social roles become more specialized.
Social-Psychological Impacts – A Double-Edged Sword:
○ Liberation & Opportunity: Cities offer freedom from ascribed identities, greater social
mobility, and exposure to diverse ideas and opportunities.
○ Stress, Anomie & Social Isolation: The fast pace, competition, anonymity, and potential
weakening of traditional community ties can also lead to psychological stress, anomie (a
sense of normlessness or social disorientation), and feelings of isolation for some urban
dwellers.
New Forms of Social Organization: Urban dwellers often form new associations based on
common interests. Digital connectivity also reshapes social networks.
Conspicuous Consumption: Urban settings can become arenas for displaying social status
through consumption.

•

•
•

Urban Lifestyles: Conspicuous Consumption in India
Conspicuous consumption is the practice of acquiring goods and services primarily to exhibit
social status and wealth, rather than for their functional utility. This trend is particularly
evident in urban environments, where visible consumption helps individuals assert their
position in diverse social hierarchies and signal aspirations.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

101

Student Notes:

In Indian cities, this manifests through choices like luxury vehicles, branded apparel, and
residences in exclusive gated communities, often associated with the new middle class. Such
consumption patterns serve as potent social markers, contribute to identity formation, and
are integral to India's expanding "culture of consumption," reflecting evolving class dynamics
and societal values.

2.2. The Evolving Urban Landscape: Patterns of Growth
India's urbanization is not monolithic; it manifests in diverse spatial patterns and hierarchies:
•

•

Metropolitan Dominance: Large metropolitan cities (Mumbai, Delhi, etc.) continue to be
major centers of population and economic activity, attracting the lion's share of investment
and migrants. Delhi was the world's second-most populous capital city in 2018 with over 28
million people.
○ This concentration leads to immense pressure on infrastructure, housing, and the
environment in these megacities.
The Rise of Tier-II and Tier-III Cities: India is witnessing the rise of a growing number of Tier
2 cities like Pune and Ahmedabad.

Corridor Development: Urban and industrial development is increasingly channeled along
major transportation and industrial corridors.
○ Example – Delhi-Mumbai Industrial Corridor (DMIC): The DMIC project aims to develop
new industrial cities, logistics hubs, and high-tech zones between Delhi and Mumbai.
Unique Regional Patterns – The Rural-Urban Continuum: In states like Kerala, urbanization
is dispersed, creating a continuous belt of urban or semi-urban settlements rather than large
cities. This creates governance and service delivery challenges across a seamless landscape.

adjoining rural or undeveloped lands.

3. Urban Society: Stratification, Inequality, and Exclusion
Urban India, while a crucible of opportunity, is also characterized by complex social hierarchies,
deep-seated inequalities, and processes of exclusion that shape the lives of its diverse
inhabitants.

3.1. Social Hierarchies in the City: Caste, Class, and Religion
Urbanization transforms, but does not eradicate, traditional social hierarchies. New forms of
stratification also emerge, creating a complex social mosaic.
Caste in Urban Settings:
•
•
•
•

Persistence and Transformation: While public manifestations of untouchability and rigid
ritual hierarchies may decline in the anonymity of cities, caste identity often transforms,
influencing social networks, political mobilization, and access to resources.
Caste as a Network: Caste and kinship ties often play a crucial role in urban migration,
helping individuals find housing, employment, and social support in city.
Discrimination and Exclusion: Caste-based discrimination persists in urban areas, notably in
the rental housing market and sometimes in employment. Studies reveal 17% of SCs live in
neighborhoods with over 80% SC population, indicating segregation.
Caste Assertion: Cities also become sites for caste assertion and identity politics, where
marginalized caste groups organize to
demand rights and challenge discrimination.
Case Study – The 'New Middle Class' in

•

•

Mobility and Markers: While cities offer

patterns, educational attainment, and residential location often become key markers of
urban class status.

Religion in Urban Life:
•

Diversity and Public Presence: Cities are typically religiously diverse. Religious institutions
(temples, mosques, churches, gurudwaras) often play a significant role in the social and
cultural life of urban communities, providing spiritual solace, community services, and
spaces for social interaction. Public celebrations of religious festivals are prominent features
of urban life.
Communalism and Polarization: Unfortunately, urban areas can also be sites of communal
tension and polarization, sometimes fueled by competition over resources, political
mobilization along religious lines, or historical grievances. Residential segregation along
religious lines is also a reality ( 26% of the Muslim population lives in neighborhoods with
over 80% Muslim population).

•

3.2. Segregation and Marginalization in Urban Spaces
Marginalization refers to the exclusion of groups from access to urban services, resources, and
decision-making processes.

Mechanisms of Segregation:
• Housing
Market
Dynamics:
Discrimination by landlords and housing
societies against certain castes, religions,
or even bachelors/single women.
• Socio-Economic Disparities: Income
levels largely determine housing choices,
naturally
leading
to
class-based
segregation.
• Social Preferences & Fear: Desire to live
among one's own community for cultural
comfort or due to perceived insecurity in
mixed localities.
• State Policies (or lack thereof):
Inadequate inclusive housing policies or
poorly planned resettlement projects can
exacerbate segregation.

Consequences:
• Unequal
Access:
Segregated
communities often face poorer access to
quality schools, healthcare, public
transport, and civic amenities (water,
sanitation).
• Limited Opportunities: Spatial isolation
can limit access to diverse employment
opportunities concentrated elsewhere in
the city.
• Reinforcement of Prejudice: Lack of
inter-group interaction can reinforce
stereotypes and hinder social cohesion.
• Political Voicelessness: Marginalized and
segregated groups may find it harder to
organize and have their voices heard in
urban governance.

Case Study – Mumbra (Thane):
Mumbra, near Mumbai, developed into a
large Muslim-concentrated settlement,
particularly after the 1992-93 communal
riots in Mumbai, as many sought safety in
numbers. While providing a sense of

Gentrification
Gentrification is the process where
wealthier individuals move into
neighborhoods traditionally occupied by
lower-income residents, resulting in
increased property values and often
displacement of the original population.

It is viewed as a dual-edged
phenomenon: on one hand, it can lead
to urban revitalization and improved infrastructure; on the other, it risks displacing long-term,
low-income residents who can no longer afford the rising costs. Gentrification is a key driver
of social inequality and economic segregation, reshaping communities and altering their
social fabric.

3.3. The Urban Poor: Livelihoods, Vulnerabilities, and Living
Conditions
A significant proportion of India's urban population comprises the urban poor, whose experience
of the city is vastly different from that of the affluent or middle classes. The urban poverty rate
was 12.55% in 2022 (IER 2024).
Livelihoods:
•
•

Primarily engaged in the informal sector: street vending, domestic work, construction,
rickshaw pulling, waste picking, small-scale manufacturing, and increasingly, the gig
economy (e.g., delivery workers).
Characterized by low wages, irregular employment, lack of job security, no social benefits
(like health insurance or pensions), and often hazardous working conditions.

Vulnerabilities:
•

•

•
•
•

Housing Insecurity & Slums: Many urban poor

population density, inadequate access to clean

Health Risks: Higher exposure to pollution, poor sanitation, and limited access to affordable
healthcare lead to greater health vulnerabilities.
Social Exclusion: Often face stigmatization and are excluded from mainstream social and
civic life.
Constant Threat of Eviction: Lack of secure tenure makes them vulnerable to forced
evictions due to city development projects or "beautification" drives.

3.4. Migrants in the City: Challenges of Integration and Access
Migrants form a substantial part of the urban population, contributing significantly to the city's
economy, yet they often face immense challenges.
Challenges Faced:
•

•

•

Economic
Precariousness:
Often

with little security. Exploitation by

difficulties due to language barriers,

discrimination from the host population.
This can lead to acculturation stress and a

Housing and Basic Amenities: Struggle to
find affordable and decent housing, often
ending up in slums or overcrowded

Case Study – Plight of Migrant Workers
During COVID-19 Lockdowns (2020):
The nationwide lockdown exposed the
extreme vulnerability of millions of
internal migrants in Indian cities. With
sudden loss of jobs and wages, no social
safety nets, and inability to pay rent, they
were forced to undertake arduous journeys
back to their villages, often on foot. This
crisis highlighted their invisibility in urban
planning and lack of access to basic rights
and social protection.

can be a major barrier to accessing PDS, banking, and government schemes.
Political Exclusion: Many internal migrants lack voting rights in their city of residence,
limiting their ability to influence local governance and demand accountability.
Health and Safety: More vulnerable to occupational hazards and illnesses due to poor living
and working conditions. Women and children migrants face additional safety concerns.

Successful integration of migrants depends on factors like duration of stay, skill levels, social
networks, and inclusive policies. However, many migrants, especially those in low-skilled jobs,
remain on the peripheries of urban society.
Intersectionality and Compounded Disadvantage
Understanding Intersectionality: Intersectionality is an analytical framework for
understanding how various social and political identities (e.g., caste, class, gender, religion,
ethnicity, sexual orientation, migrant status) combine or "intersect" to create unique modes
of discrimination and privilege. It emphasizes that disadvantage is often compounded when
an individual belongs to multiple marginalized groups.
Relevance in Urban India: In the complex social fabric of Indian cities, intersectionality helps
reveal layers of vulnerability that might otherwise remain hidden:
•
•
•

A Dalit woman migrant working as domestic help faces caste discrimination, gender
exploitation, class vulnerability, and migrant insecurities—different from a middle-class
woman or a Dalit man.
A Muslim migrant from a poor rural background in a communally polarized urban area
might face compounded discrimination based on religion, class, and migrant status.
Street children face overlapping vulnerabilities related to age, poverty, lack of adult
protection, and sometimes caste or ethnicity.

Implications for Policy: The concept moves beyond single-axis thinking (e.g., policies just for
'women' or 'the poor') to design more targeted and effective interventions addressing
specific, compounded disadvantages. This ensures urban development strives for inclusivity
and social justice.

4. Urban Impact on Social Institutions and Cultural Life
4.1. The Changing Urban Family, Marriage, and Kinship
The urban environment, with its emphasis on mobility and individualism, significantly impacts
traditional family structures and practices.
•

•

•

Family Structures:
○ There's a noticeable shift from joint family systems towards nuclear families, especially
in cities due to factors like housing constraints, occupational mobility, and a desire for
individual autonomy. The proportion of nuclear families is higher in urban areas (around
52%) compared to rural areas (around 37%).
○ However, functional jointness often persists, where families living separately continue
to provide mutual financial, emotional, and ritualistic support.
○ Cities also see greater acceptance of diverse family forms, including single-parent
households and dual-income couples.
Marriage Patterns:
○ The age of marriage tends to be higher in urban areas, particularly for women, often
linked to higher educational attainment and career aspirations. The median age at first
marriage for urban women (20-49 years) was 22.1 years compared to 19.8 years for rural
women (NFHS-5).
○ There is increased individual agency in mate selection, with a rise in love marriages
alongside traditionally arranged ones.
○ Cities exhibit higher prevalence of inter-caste and inter-religious marriages (around 5%
in urban vs. 2% in rural), reflecting greater social mixing and changing attitudes.
○ Divorce and remarriage, while still carrying social stigma, are relatively more visible and
accepted in urban settings.
Kinship Ties:
○ Day-to-day dependence on wider kin may reduce, but kinship networks remain crucial
for life-cycle rituals, emergency support, and navigating urban systems (e.g., finding jobs
or housing, especially for new migrants).
○ Caste and community associations in cities often evolve to fulfill social and supportive
functions previously handled by extended kin.

4.2. Women in the City: Opportunities, Challenges, and Agency
Urbanization offers both significant opportunities and distinct challenges for women.
•

•

Opportunities:
○ Education and Employment: Cities provide
greater access to higher education and a
wider range of employment for women, from
formal sector jobs to roles in the informal
economy. This can lead to economic
independence and empowerment.
○ Social and Political Space: Urban
environments can offer women greater
freedom from patriarchal constraints,
increased public visibility, and more avenues
for social and political participation.
Challenges:
○ Safety and Security: A major concern is
safety in public spaces, transport, and
workplaces. Cities often report high rates of
crimes against women.

Case Study – Garment Factory
Workers in Bengaluru/Tiruppur:
Many women, often migrants, work
in garment export factories in urban
hubs like Bengaluru and Tiruppur.
While these jobs provide crucial
income, studies highlight issues like
low wages, long working hours, poor
working conditions, and challenges in
accessing proper housing and
childcare.
Despite challenges urban women
increasingly asserting their rights in
private and public spheres.

Dual Burden: Working women frequently shoulder the "dual burden" of professional
work and household responsibilities, often without adequate support systems like
affordable childcare.
Workplace Issues: Challenges include the gender pay gap, limited career advancement
(glass ceiling), and workplace harassment.
Housing Difficulties: Single women often face discrimination and difficulties finding safe
and affordable housing.

Moral Policing and Restrictions on Women’s Freedom in Metropolitan Cities
There has been a troubling rise in moral
policing in metropolitan areas, where
women’s choices—especially regarding
their appearance and clothing—are
frequently challenged on moral grounds.
Independent and assertive women often
face various forms of violence and
harassment.
Female tenants, in particular, must conform
to strict codes of conduct to assure
landlords of their suitability and maintain
good relations with neighbours. This reflects a broader societal pattern that links women’s
sexuality to family honour, leading to the restriction of women’s freedom and independence
within a controlling moral framework.

The urban environment presents a mixed bag of prospects and problems for children.

○ Generally better access to a wider range of educational institutions and specialized

○ Exposure to diverse information, technology, and extracurricular opportunities.
Problems:
○ Vulnerabilities of Urban Poor Children: Children in slums and low-income households

living conditions, and lack of safe play spaces. The issue of street children lacking
protection and basic amenities is
a stark urban reality.

Many Indian cities struggle with inadequate
basic infrastructure, severely impacting quality
of life and exacerbating inequalities.
•

Case Study – Pune's SWaCH Model:

and recognition of their contribution.

The crisis exposed the extreme

scarcity caused by erratic monsoons, overextraction
of

and
inadequate
management.
Residents

disproportionately impacted.

many urban poor lack household toilets.

bodies.

congestion

Congestion charge
It is a charge imposed on users of
specific, over-crowded roads, to ease
congestion and vehicular emissions
during peak traffic hours by reducing
demand and usage. For this purpose,
cities demarcate a “congestion zone”
or multiple “congestion zones”.
India’s biggest cities have greater
traffic congestion than New York, but
none of these cities impose
congestion charges. A 2018 proposal
made by the Delhi Lt. Governor in this
regard has not been acted upon.

These deficits cause daily hardships, lost productivity, higher health costs, and significant time
spent accessing services, disproportionately affecting women (who often collect water) and the
poor.

Urban environmental degradation is not only ecological but also a profound social challenge with
inequitable consequences.
•

○ Disproportionate Impact: The urban poor and marginalized communities often live in the

environmental health risks.
○ Health Consequences: High rates of respiratory illnesses, waterborne diseases, and other

○ Impact on Livelihoods: Degradation of commons like rivers and lakes destroys livelihoods
dependent on them (e.g., fishing).

•

5.4. Overcrowding in Tier-1 and 2 cities
Large cities in India suffer from huge population density and lack of adequate public spaces.
•
•

85-90% of households in these cities make do with just one or two rooms, often cramming
five to six people into a single space (Census of India, 2011).
Traffic Congestion Index 2023 ranked Bengaluru the most congested city in India and the
sixth-most congested in the world.

5.5. High cost of living
•

•

All large cities suffer from the ‘employability’ and ‘livability’ paradox. Across many cities
and regions, housing prices and rental rates have soared, making it increasingly difficult for
individuals to find affordable options. Also, transport, healthcare and education costs in such
cities are high. Low-wage workers and individuals on fixed and stagnant incomes often find
it challenging to keep pace with the rising costs, resulting in reduced purchasing power and
compromised quality of life.
According to Mercer’s Cost of Living City rankings (2024), in India, Mumbai ranks the highest
followed by Delhi.

During 2023, property prices in India’s 13 largest cities rose 18.8 percent over a year. Regarding
residential land, Mumbai, Delhi, Kolkata, Bengaluru, Pune, Chennai, Hyderabad, Ahmedabad,
Jaipur, and Surat were the 10 costliest Indian cities.

5.6. Urban Social Issues
Cities are sites of complex social issues requiring nuanced understanding.
•

Urban Crime: Metropolitan cities report significant shares of cognizable crimes including
crimes against women, property crimes, and emerging cybercrimes.
○ As of 2022, the 19 metropolitan cities, which make up 8.12% of India's population,
account for 14.65% of the country's cognizable crimes (NCRB 2022).

•

Anomie and Social Disorganization: Classical theories linked urban anonymity and weak
social ties to anomie and social disorganization.
○ However, contemporary research shows cities possess their own social organization
with strong informal networks, community bonds, and associational life thriving in
neighborhoods or migrant groups.

6. Governing and Planning Urban India for the Future
The future of India is inextricably linked to the future of its cities. Effective governance, robust
planning, and a commitment to inclusivity and sustainability are paramount to navigating the
challenges and harnessing the opportunities of continued urbanization.

6.1. Urban Governance in India: Structures, Challenges, and
Reforms
Effective urban governance is the cornerstone of well-managed, livable cities. However, it
remains a significant area of concern in India.
•

•
•
•
•
•
•
•

Structure of Urban Governance: ULBs are primary institutions for local self-governance, but
urban administration often involves multiple agencies, including state-level parastatal bodies
(e.g., Development Authorities, Water Supply and Sewerage Boards, Housing Boards), leading
to fragmented responsibilities and coordination challenges.
Key Challenges in Urban Governance: The "3Fs" Deficit – Funds, Functions, Functionaries:
Funds: ULBs suffer from weak fiscal autonomy, limited revenue-generating powers (heavy
reliance on property tax, often poorly administered), and inadequate untied fund transfers
from states. SFC recommendations are often not fully implemented.
Functions: Despite the 12th Schedule listing 18 functions for devolution, actual transfer by
states is partial and often unfunded.
Functionaries: Severe shortage of qualified technical and managerial staff hinders planning
and service delivery.
Weak Planning and Enforcement: Master plans are often outdated, poorly implemented, or
violated due to weak enforcement.
Limited Citizen Participation: Genuine participation in planning and decision-making is often
inadequate or tokenistic, despite encouragement of social audits and public hearings.
Lack of Coordination: Overlapping jurisdictions between ULBs and parastatal agencies create
confusion and hamper coordinated action.

7. Strategies for Managing Future Urbanization in India
India's rapid urbanization presents a complex challenge. Here are some key strategies to ensure
sustainable and inclusive growth:

7.1. Strengthening Urban Planning and Governance
•

Mixed-Use Development: Promoting mixed-use developments that integrate residential,
commercial, and recreational spaces within peri-urban areas can create more vibrant and
self-contained communities, reducing reliance on travel and improving quality of life.
Infrastructure Investment in Peri-Urban Areas: Investing in infrastructure like water supply,
sanitation, and waste management systems in peri-urban areas can make these areas more
attractive for housing development, offering a viable alternative to congested city centers.
Decentralised development: Most of the Union Budget for urban development should be
devolved as direct transfers to the States and to city governments.
Citizen Participation: Encourage active citizen participation in urban planning processes.
Public hearings and feedback mechanisms ensure residents have a say in shaping their cities.

•
•
•

Case Study: The Baan Mankong (or “secure housing”) project in Thailand, has been extremely
successful in addressing the housing problems of Bangkok’s poorest urban citizens.
• Baan Mankong (“secure housing”) initiative was launched in 2003 to address housing
issues through a holistic community development process, while tackling structural issues
of poverty, governance, and political participation.
• CODI (Community Organization Development Institute, a quasi-governmental
organization) created a platform for the inclusion of informal settlers in the planning
process. The success of this program has been largely credited to putting the city’s
slum dwellers at the centre of the policy making process.

•

Focus on Tier 2 and 3 Cities: Invest in the development of smaller cities (Tier 2 and 3) to
create a more balanced urban network. This reduces pressure on megacities by offering
alternative destinations for migrants seeking opportunities.

7.3. Transit-Oriented Development (TOD)
Urbanization has led to horizontal growth of the cities, thus creating problems of urban sprawl.
This has resulted in an increase in trip lengths and higher usage of private vehicles, problems of
pollution and increased demand for infrastructure.
Transit-Oriented Development (TOD): Promise and Challenges for Indian Cities
•

What is TOD?
An urban planning strategy to create vibrant, livable, and sustainable communities
centered around high-quality public transport (metro, BRT, suburban rail).
TOD integrates land use and transport planning and aims to develop planned sustainable
urban growth centers, having walkable and livable communes with high density mixed
land-use. Citizens have access to open green and public spaces and at the same time
transit facilities are efficiently utilized.
Key features:

•

Case Study – Bengaluru's Namma Metro:
A prominent example of Transit-Oriented Development (TOD) in India is the Bengaluru's
Namma Metro. The development around the metro stations, especially in areas like MG
Road, Indiranagar, and Byappanahalli, showcases TOD principles. The development
around the metro stations, especially in areas like MG Road, Indiranagar, and
Byappanahalli, showcases TOD principles.

Effective TOD requires a holistic approach, political will, and participatory planning to ensure
benefits are equitable and sustainable.

CHAPTER 6: EFFECTS OF GLOBALISATION ON INDIAN
SOCIETY
Key Data & Facts at a Glance
•

•

•

Economic Integration & Flows:
○ Trade Openness: India's Trade-to-GDP ratio increased substantially from just over 10%
in the 1990s to around 45% in recent years, indicating deep integration with the global
economy.
○ Foreign Direct Investment (FDI): FDI inflows have surged post-liberalization, reaching a
record high of over $84 billion in 2021-22.
○ Remittances: India is the world's largest recipient of remittances, receiving an estimated
$125 billion in 2023 (World Bank), showcasing the economic importance of the Indian
diaspora.
Digital Globalization:
○ Internet Penetration: Has grown exponentially, reaching over 900 million users in India
by 2025 (IAMAI-Kantar Report projection), forming the backbone of the digital economy.
○ Global IT Hub: India accounts for over 55% of the world's IT-BPM (Business Process
Management) outsourcing market, a direct outcome of globalization.
Socio-Economic Impact:
○ Gig Economy Workforce: India's gig economy is projected to expand to 23.5 million
workers by 2029-30 (NITI Aayog), highlighting a major shift in work patterns.
○ Youth Unemployment: Over 80% of unemployed Indians are youth, indicating the
challenge of "jobless growth" where economic expansion doesn't translate into
sufficient job creation for the burgeoning population.
○ Urbanization: Globalization is a key driver of urbanization. India's urban population was
31.16% (Census 2011) and is rapidly increasing, creating both opportunities and
challenges.
○ Inequality: Globalization is often linked to rising inequality. An Oxfam report highlighted
that India's top 1% own more than 40.5% of the nation's total wealth (2021).

Glossary of Essential Concepts
•
•
•
•
•
•
•

Globalization: The process of increasing interdependence and integration of economies,
societies, and cultures, driven by cross-border flows of goods, capital, technology, ideas, and
people.
Cultural Homogenization: The process by which local cultures are eroded and replaced by a
dominant, uniform global culture, often associated with Westernization.
Glocalization: The adaptation of global products and ideas to suit local cultures. It is a twoway process representing the "mixing of the global with the local".
Cultural Hybridity: The creation of new and unique cultural forms from the fusion of
different cultures (e.g., 'Hinglish', Indian fusion music).
Time-Space Compression: The process by which the speeding up of economic and social
processes, driven by technology, effectively "shrinks" geographical distance and time,
intensifying global connections.
Neoliberalism: The dominant ideology underpinning modern economic globalization. It
advocates for policies such as privatization, deregulation, free trade, and reducing the role
of the state in the economy.
Deglobalization: The process of diminishing interdependence and integration between
nations, often marked by rising protectionism, trade wars, and a focus on national selfreliance.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

124

www.visionias.in

# 8468022022

©Vision IAS

Student Notes:

•
•
•
•
•
•
•
•

Gig Economy: A labor market characterized by short-term contracts and freelance work,
enabled by digital platforms. It represents a shift from traditional, permanent employment
to a more flexible but often precarious form of work.
Cosmopolitanism: An outlook where individuals see themselves as "citizens of the world,"
with ethical and cultural allegiances that transcend national borders.
Consumerism / Culture of Consumption: A social and economic order that encourages the
acquisition of goods and services in ever-increasing amounts, where consumption becomes
a key source of identity and social status.
Cultural Imperialism: A more critical term for homogenization, it implies that a dominant
culture (often Western) actively imposes its values, products, and norms on less powerful
societies, undermining local traditions.
Deterritorialization: The weakening of the link between culture and a specific geographical
place. Global media and migration mean that one no longer needs to live in India to
experience Indian culture, and vice versa.
Globalization 4.0: The current and future wave of globalization driven not by goods or
services, but by cutting-edge technologies like Artificial Intelligence (AI), the Internet of
Things (IoT), Big Data, and biotechnology.
Data Colonialism: A critical perspective arguing that the data of individuals and nations in
the developing world is being harvested, controlled, and monetized by a few global tech
giants, creating a new form of colonial dependency.
Platform Capitalism: The business model of companies like Uber, Amazon, and Zomato,
which do not own the primary assets but control the digital platform that connects producers
and consumers, thereby capturing immense value.

Introduction
Globalization is a multi-dimensional process of increasing interdependence,
interconnectedness, and integration of economies and societies across the globe.
Driven by advancements in technology, communication, and transport, it has led to a time-space
compression, where distances shrink and events in one part of the world can have an immediate
impact elsewhere.
For India, the process accelerated dramatically after the Liberalisation, Privatisation, and
Globalization (LPG) reforms of 1991. This watershed moment transitioned India from a relatively
closed, state-controlled economy to one that is increasingly integrated with the global system.

Student Notes:

The impact of globalization on Indian society is complex and paradoxical. It has unleashed new
economic opportunities and fostered a more cosmopolitan outlook, but it has also created deep
social anxieties, exacerbated inequalities, and posed significant challenges to traditional
institutions and cultural identities.

1. The Cultural Sphere: Identity, Values, and Consumption
The cultural impact of globalization is one of its most visible and debated aspects. It has led to a
dynamic interplay between global influences and local traditions, profoundly shaping Indian
identity, values, and lifestyles.

1.1. Shifting Social Landscape
•

Linguistic Transformation: The rise of English, driven by multinational corporations and a
service sector seeking skilled English speakers, has become prominent in urban areas.
Globalization has made the English language a predator language. The English language is
a “killer” language.
Many computers have only American English software and it is the main transnational
language used in various fields of scientific, cultural, economic and business activities.

•

Weakening Caste System: Globalization has challenged traditional social hierarchies.
Increased economic opportunities and new job types have enabled social mobility, leading
to more inter-caste marriages and a gradual weakening of the caste system.

1.2. Religious Transformations
•

Within Social Structures: Globalization has facilitated a greater exchange of religious ideas
and practices. Movements against exploitative practices like Devadasi and Triple Talaq have
gained momentum due to increased awareness.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

126

www.visionias.in

# 8468022022

©Vision IAS

•

Religious Pluralism: Globalization fosters a culture of religious pluralism, where diverse
faiths interact and learn from each other. Technology allows for wider dissemination of
religious teachings, emphasizing humane and pluralistic values.

1.3. Evolving Lifestyles
•

Food Trends: The rise of global fast-food chains has introduced new eating habits, raising
concerns about health problems like obesity and diabetes.
McDonaldization
This is the process by which the principles of fast food restaurant chain McDonald’s are
fast bringing the entire world into their fold. This include:
•
•
•

Efficiency (expressed in the declining gap between placing the order and its
execution);
Calculability (indicated by the calculation by a consumer of costs in terms of money,
time and effort rather than quality of the product);
Predictability (standardizing the products so that the trust of the consumer is won);

This results in the growing tendency towards the unification of lifestyles, cultural symbols
and the modes of behaviour.
•

Fashion Choices: Western attire like jeans has become more common, particularly in
urban areas, while traditional clothing is reserved for special occasions.

1.4. The Core Cultural Debate: Homogenization vs. Hybridity
A central fear surrounding globalization is that it leads to cultural homogenization, where a
dominant, often Western, culture erodes and replaces local cultures, creating a uniform global
monoculture.
•

The Argument for Homogenization:
○ Dominance of Western Media
& Brands: The proliferation of
international fast-food chains
(like

global

and

local tastes and create a

○ Primacy of English:
The role of English as
the global language
of business and
technology can lead
to a decline in the
use and status of
regional
Indian

cultural identities.

•

The Indian Reality: Glocalization and Cultural Hybridity:
○ In reality, globalization in India is not a one-way street. The process is better understood
through the concepts of glocalization and hybridity.
○ Glocalization is the adaptation of global products to suit local cultures. This is a
deliberate strategy by global firms to enhance marketability. A classic example is
McDonald's offering the "McAloo Tikki" burger or going vegetarian during the Navratri
festival to cater to Indian tastes and sensibilities.
○ Cultural Hybridity refers to the creation of new, mixed cultural forms from the fusion of
global and local elements. This is evident everywhere in modern India, from the
popularity of 'Indi-pop' and fusion music to the widespread use of 'Hinglish' in urban
conversation and advertising.

Case Study: The Global Rise of Yoga and Ayurveda
The flow of culture is not unidirectional. Globalization has provided a massive platform for the
global export of Indian culture.
➢ Yoga, once a niche spiritual practice, has been successfully packaged and marketed
worldwide, becoming a multi-billion dollar global wellness industry. The UN's
declaration of an International Yoga Day is a testament to its global acceptance.
➢ Similarly, Ayurveda and traditional Indian wellness practices are gaining international
recognition. This phenomenon of "reverse cultural flow" challenges the simple
narrative of Western cultural imperialism.
Are We Losing Local Identity for a Global One
The question of whether globalization erodes local identity is complex. It is not a zero-sum
game where one identity replaces another. Instead, globalization has led to the formation of
multiple, layered identities.
•

•

Coexistence of Identities: An individual in modern India can simultaneously hold and
express multiple identities.
○ For example, a young software engineer in Bengaluru can be a proud Kannadiga, an
Indian, and a 'global citizen' who follows international tech trends, listens to global
music, and works for a multinational corporation.
Assertion of Local Identity: Paradoxically, the threat of a uniform global culture often
leads to a conscious assertion and revival of local identities.
○ As people are exposed to global trends, there is often a renewed interest in preserving
and promoting one's own unique language, cuisine, festivals, and traditions as a
marker of distinction and pride. This has been called the "revival of culture".

Therefore, rather than a simple loss of identity, we are witnessing a complex negotiation
where identities are constantly being redefined in relation to both local and global contexts.

1.5. The Rise of a New "Culture of Consumption"
Globalization has been a key driver in promoting a culture of consumption, particularly among
India's expanding urban middle class.
•
•

Drivers: This shift is fuelled by rising disposable incomes from new service sector jobs, the
easy availability of credit, and the pervasive influence of global media and advertising, which
create new aspirations and desires.
Manifestations: The proliferation of shopping malls, multiplexes, and global brand outlets
in Indian cities are physical manifestations of this new culture. Consumption is increasingly
seen as a primary form of self-expression and a key marker of social status.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI

Student Notes:

•

Social Differentiation: This culture creates new forms of social differentiation based on
'taste', 'fashion', and 'lifestyle' choices, rather than just traditional markers like caste. Owning
the latest smartphone or driving a certain brand of car becomes a signal of one's position in
the new social hierarchy.
Case Study: OTT Platforms and Cultural Flows
Over-the-Top (OTT) platforms like Netflix, Amazon Prime, and Hotstar are powerful
contemporary agents of cultural globalization in India. They exemplify its dual nature:
•
•

Global to Local: They bring a vast library of international content—from Hollywood
blockbusters to Korean dramas and Turkish series—directly into Indian homes,
influencing tastes and cultural conversations.
Local to Global: Simultaneously, these platforms have provided an unprecedented global
stage for Indian, especially regional, content. Critically acclaimed Malayalam, Tamil, and
Marathi films and series have found a global audience, showcasing the richness of India's
diverse cinematic traditions and creating a powerful "soft power" narrative.

2. Impact on Social Institutions & Specific Groups
Globalization's influence permeates the most personal aspects of life, transforming core social
institutions like family and marriage, and affecting different demographic groups in unique and
often contradictory ways.

2.1. Globalization-Specific Changes in Family & Marriage
While the structural shift from joint to nuclear families has been discussed in the chapter on
Salient Feature of Indian Society, this section focuses on new dynamics and forms specifically
accelerated by globalization.
•

•

The Rise of the "Transnational Family": The global demand for skilled Indian labor, especially
in the IT and service sectors, has led to large-scale international migration. This has given rise
to the transnational family, where members live across different continents.
○ Dynamics: These families are characterized by economic prosperity and global exposure
on one hand, and emotional distance, challenges in caregiving, and new forms of
negotiation on the other.
○ Technology as Social Glue: Information and Communication Technology (ICT) plays a
crucial role. Video calls, instant messaging, and social media act as a "digital glue,"
allowing for a semblance of "virtual co-presence" and helping maintain kinship ties
across vast geographical distances.
○ Social & Emotional Impact:
> The "Skype Parent": This phenomenon creates new challenges in socialization and
emotional bonding for children.
> Burden on Grandparents: Grandparents in India often step in as primary caregivers,
leading to inter-generational strain.
> Fragmented Identities: Children may develop bi-cultural or "third-culture"
identities, navigating between the culture of their parents' home country and their
country of residence.
Changing Aspirations in Marriage: Global media, including films, television series, and social
media, has exposed Indian society to diverse ideas about relationships and marriage. This
has fueled a shift in aspirations, particularly among the urban youth, towards values of
individualism, romantic love, and companionship, often leading to conflict with traditional
family expectations of arranged, endogamous marriages.

2.2. Impact on Specific Social Groups
Globalization affects different groups in society unevenly.
•

Impact on Women :
The impact has been a "double-edged sword".
Opportunities:
○

○
○

Globalization has created new job opportunities in sectors like BPOs, IT, and exportoriented manufacturing, providing many women with economic independence and
greater agency for the first time.
The migration of skilled, unmarried women to cities for these jobs has often increased
their personal freedom and delayed the age of marriage.
It has also fueled feminist movements through exposure to global ideas.

Challenges:
○
○
•

It has also led to the feminization of labor in low-wage, exploitative conditions and has
intensified the "double burden" of paid work and unpaid care work.
Furthermore, digital globalization has exposed women to new forms of tech-facilitated
gender-based violence (TFGBV), including cyberstalking and online harassment.

Impact on the Elderly:
○

○

Challenges: The migration of children for global careers has weakened the traditional
joint family support system, leading to increased feelings of loneliness, neglect, and
emotional vulnerability among the elderly.
New Risks & Opportunities: They face new risks like financial insecurity and elder abuse.
Conversely, technology offers new ways to stay connected, and for the affluent,
globalization provides access to advanced medical treatments from around the world.

Case Study: The 'Lonely Elderly' of
Kerala
Kerala, a state with one of the highest
rates of international migration, is
facing a severe social crisis of "empty
nests." In Kerala,an estimated 6%
elderly parents live alone, financially
supported by remittances from their
children in the Gulf or the West.
However, studies and reports
highlight that this financial security
does not compensate for the lack of
physical support and the profound
emotional loneliness. This has led to
a rising demand for old age homes
and paid-care services, marking a
significant shift in the state's social
fabric.
•

Impact on the Youth:
○ Opportunities: Globalization has provided Indian youth with unprecedented access to
global education (online courses, study abroad programs), new-age careers (IT, digital
marketing, startups), and a cosmopolitan outlook through cultural exposure.
○ Challenges: The Aspiration-Opportunity Mismatch:
> "Jobless Growth": Global exposure creates high career aspirations, but the servicesled growth model has not created enough jobs, leading to high unemployment
among educated youth.
> Identity Crisis: The tension between the traditional values of their parents and the
global consumerist culture they are exposed to can lead to an identity crisis and
inter-generational conflict.
> Mental Health: Intense competition, career anxiety, and social media pressures
contribute to rising mental health issues.
Case Study: The "Kota Factory" Phenomenon
The intense pressure on students in coaching
hubs like Kota is a societal consequence
amplified by globalization. The competition is
for a limited number of seats in elite
institutions (like IITs) that are seen as
gateways to high-paying, often global,
careers.
This globalization-fueled aspiration, when met
with immense competition and familial
pressure, creates severe mental health
challenges and stress, leading to tragic
instances of student suicides that are
frequently reported in the news.

Impact on Children:
○ Positive Impacts: Children today have unprecedented access to global information and
diverse educational resources through the internet and digital platforms.
○ Negative Impacts: The most significant negative impact is on the process of
socialization.
○ Digital Intrusion: Excessive screen time is replacing crucial face-to-face interaction with
family members, impacting emotional development and social skills.
○ Consumer Culture: They are the primary targets of global advertising, fostering a
premature desire for branded goods and unhealthy food habits.
Impact on the Tribal Population: For India's tribal communities, globalization has often been
a story of marginalization.
○ Economic Opportunities: In some cases, globalization has created global markets for
unique tribal handicrafts, art, and organic produce, offering a potential source of income.
○ Threat of Resource Exploitation & Displacement: More commonly, the global demand
for minerals and resources has led to the large-scale acquisition of tribal lands (jal,
jangal, zameen) by multinational corporations (MNCs) for mining and industrial projects.
This results in development-led displacement, loss of traditional livelihoods, and the
erosion of their unique cultural identities.

Case Study: The Dongria Kondh and Vedanta Mining
The struggle of the Dongria Kondh tribe in
Odisha's Niyamgiri hills is a classic case of a local
community resisting corporate globalization.
The tribe fought a decade-long battle against
UK-based mining giant Vedanta, which planned
to mine bauxite on their sacred mountain.
The movement gained international attention
and, in a landmark decision, the Supreme Court
of India empowered local village councils (Gram
Sabhas) to decide the project's fate, which they
unanimously rejected. This case highlights the intense conflict between global capital and
indigenous rights.

3. The Economic Sphere: Work, Class, and Agriculture
The most palpable impact of globalization has been on the economic structure of India.
Economic Aspects of Globalization

3.1. Transformation of Work & Emergence of the New Middle Class
•

The Service Sector Boom: Globalization, powered by the ICT revolution, catalyzed a massive
shift in the Indian economy towards the service sector. India leveraged its large pool of
English-speaking, skilled manpower to become the "back office of the world."
○ Data: The IT-BPM industry became a cornerstone, directly employing over 4.5 million
people and contributing significantly to India's GDP and exports.
The New Middle Class: This service sector boom was the primary engine for the creation of
India's new middle class.
○ Characteristics: This class is largely urban, educated, aspirational, and deeply integrated
with the global culture of consumption. Its identity is shaped by professional aspirations
and access to global brands and lifestyles.
○ Social Impact: This class has reshaped urban landscapes, fueled the growth of consumer
markets (malls, e-commerce), and influenced social values related to individualism and
career orientation.
The Challenge of "Jobless Growth": A major critique of India's globalization-led growth
model is its failure to create sufficient employment.
○ The high-end, services-led model has created wealth for a small set of highly-skilled
individuals but has not generated enough jobs for the vast majority of India's youth.
○ Data from the Periodic Labour Force Survey (PLFS) has shown that only 21.7% of India's
workers have regular salaried jobs, with most being casual laborers or self-employed,
highlighting the precarity for the masses.

•

•

Case Study: The Indian Startup "Hustle Culture"
Fueled by global venture capital, India's startup ecosystem (e.g., in Bengaluru, Gurugram) has
created exciting opportunities for youth. However, it is also criticized for promoting a toxic
"hustle culture."
Reports and employee testimonials highlight immense pressure, unsustainable work hours,
and a high risk of burnout. The constant uncertainty of funding and viability takes a significant
toll on the mental health of founders and employees, showcasing the stressful side of
participating in the high-stakes global innovation economy.

3.2. The Gig Economy and the Future of Work

Student Notes:

The gig economy, characterized by short-term, platform-based freelance work, is a direct
product of digital globalization.
•

Scale: According to a NITI Aayog report, India's gig workforce is expected to expand to 23.5
million workers by 2029-30, signifying a fundamental shift in the nature of work.
A Double-Edged Sword: Empowerment vs. Precarity:
○ Empowerment: The primary appeal is flexibility, which allows workers, especially
women, to earn an income while managing household and care responsibilities. It lowers
entry barriers and provides autonomy over work hours.
○ Precarity: This flexibility comes at a cost. Gig workers are typically classified as
"independent contractors," denying them social security, health insurance, minimum
wage protection, and paid leave. They face income instability, algorithmic management,
and significant safety concerns.

•

Case Study: The Platform Food-Delivery Worker
The life of a food delivery worker for platforms like Zomato or Swiggy exemplifies the gig
economy's reality.
•
•

The Promise: The platform offers the promise of flexible earnings—the ability to "be your
own boss."
The Reality: Workers often face long hours in hazardous traffic, low pay-per-delivery, high
fuel costs, and the pressure of a rating system. They are controlled by an algorithm with
no human interface for grievance redressal, highlighting a new form of digital vulnerability.

3.3. Impact on the Agrarian Sector
Globalization has deeply impacted India's agricultural sector, often negatively affecting its most
vulnerable stakeholders.
•
•

Shift in Cropping Patterns: The integration with global markets encouraged a shift from
cultivating traditional subsistence crops (like millets) to cash crops for export (e.g., cotton,
tobacco, sugarcane), often impacting local food security.
Challenges for Small Farmers:
○ Competition: Small and marginal farmers, who dominate Indian agriculture, find it
impossible to compete with heavily subsidized agricultural imports from developed
countries.
○ Dependence on MNCs: The push for high-yield varieties has increased dependence on
multinational corporations for expensive patented seeds (e.g., Bt Cotton), fertilizers, and
pesticides, leading to rising input costs and debt.
○ Global Price Volatility: Exposure to the fluctuations of global commodity markets makes
farmers vulnerable. A crash in global prices can lead to massive losses, as domestic price
support mechanisms (like MSP) have their own limitations.
○ This combination of factors has been a major contributor to the ongoing crisis of agrarian
distress and farmer suicides in India.

Globalization and Inequality: The K-Shaped Impact
Concept: A "K-shaped recovery" is a powerful metaphor to describe the unequal economic
trajectory that often follows a major shock. Instead of a uniform recovery for all (V-shape),
different parts of society recover at vastly different rates, creating a diverging "K" pattern.
Globalization's K-Shaped Impact on India:
The long-term impact of globalization on Indian society can be seen as a continuous K-shaped
process:

The Upper Arm
(Soaring):
This
represents
the
globally-connected

Industries like ITBPM,

The Lower Arm (Stagnating): This represents the informal sector and traditional
agriculture. These sectors, employing the vast majority of Indians, have faced stagnation
or decline. They have been hit by competition from cheap imports, displacement by large
corporations, and have not been able to integrate into the global value chains on favorable
terms.

This framework helps to analytically explain the central paradox of globalization in India: the
simultaneous existence of booming stock markets and world-class tech hubs alongside
persistent agrarian distress and high unemployment. It shows how the integration process
itself can deepen pre-existing inequalities.

4. Emerging Debates of Globalization and India's Future
The trajectory of globalization is not linear. It is constantly evolving, facing new challenges, and
sparking new debates.

4.1. Globalization 4.0: Data, Currency, and Disinformation

Student Notes:

Globalization 4.0 refers to the next phase of globalization, driven not by physical goods, but by
intangible flows of data and knowledge.
•
•

•

•

•

It is powered by cutting-edge technologies like Artificial Intelligence (AI), the Internet of
Things (IoT), Big Data, and biotechnology.
This wave is harder to stop with traditional protectionist measures like tariffs and is creating
a hyper-connected global digital economy.

Cryptocurrency & Global Finance: Cryptocurrencies represent a form of decentralized,
borderless finance that challenges the monetary sovereignty of nation-states. While they
promise faster cross-border transactions, they also pose significant risks of volatility, use in
illicit financing, and high energy consumption, creating a major regulatory dilemma for India.
Data as a Geopolitical Resource: In the new economy, data is often called the "new oil."
There is a growing debate around "Data Colonialism," where the vast data of Indian citizens
is controlled and monetized by global tech giants. This has prompted a push for data
localization and digital sovereignty.
The Infodemic: Global social media platforms have become conduits for the rapid spread of
disinformation and hate speech, which can threaten social cohesion and democratic stability
in a diverse country like India.

Case Study: India's IT Rules, 2021
The introduction of the Information Technology (Intermediary Guidelines and Digital Media
Ethics Code) Rules, 2021, was a significant move by the Indian government to regulate global
social media platforms like Twitter (now X), Facebook, and WhatsApp. The rules mandated the
appointment of local grievance officers and traceability of messages. This represents a clear
assertion of digital sovereignty and an attempt by the state to hold global tech companies
accountable for the content on their platforms within Indian territory.

4.4. The Geopolitical Frontier: Deglobalization & Strategic Realignment
A powerful backlash against hyper-globalization has led to a trend of deglobalization.
•
•

Drivers: This is driven by rising protectionism in the West (e.g., US-China trade war), the
vulnerabilities of global supply chains exposed by the pandemic, and national security
concerns.
India's Strategy: A Balancing Act:
India's future success depends on strategically navigating this duality. The path forward
involves:
1. Embracing Globalization 4.0: Actively participating in and shaping the global digital
economy, developing AI capabilities, and leveraging its skilled workforce for high-end
digital services. This is crucial for economic growth.
2. Strategic Deglobalization: Simultaneously pursuing "Atmanirbhar Bharat" in critical
sectors like defense, semiconductors, and pharmaceuticals to build national resilience
and reduce vulnerabilities.

This balanced approach aims to harness the benefits of digital integration while insulating the
economy from the shocks of geopolitical fragmentation.

4.5. The Health Frontier: Pandemics and Global Biosecurity
The COVID-19 pandemic was a stark reminder of how deeply interconnected the world is.
•
•
•

Rapid Spread: Globalization, through international travel, enabled the virus to spread from
a local outbreak to a global pandemic within weeks.
Global Collaboration: At the same time, it enabled an unprecedented level of global
scientific collaboration. Scientists across the world shared data, and the rapid development
and deployment of vaccines was a global effort.
India as "Pharmacy of the World": The crisis highlighted India's crucial role in global
pharmaceutical supply chains, even as it exposed vulnerabilities in its own healthcare
infrastructure. The pandemic has firmly placed global health security as a key agenda item
for international cooperation.

Case Study: The COVID-19 Pandemic as a Globalization Stress Test
The COVID-19 pandemic was a watershed event for globalization. It simultaneously revealed
its fragilities and accelerated its transformation:
•
•

Exposed Vulnerability: The world witnessed the breakdown of global supply chains for
essential goods like pharmaceuticals (APIs) and personal protective equipment (PPE),
forcing nations to look inwards and focus on self-reliance.
Accelerated Digital Globalization: Simultaneously, lockdowns triggered a massive shift
towards remote work (WFH), e-commerce, digital payments, and online education,
massively accelerating the digital dimension of globalization. The pandemic thus acted as
both a brake on physical globalization and a powerful accelerator for digital globalization.

critical consciousness directed at challenging traditional power structures and biases.

inaccessible to the vast majority of Indians.
○ It can sometimes lead to performative activism ("slacktivism") on social media rather
than driving genuine, on-the-ground social change.
○ It may lead to social polarization by over-emphasizing identity differences over
universal values.

CHAPTER 7: COMMUNALISM, SECULARISM & REGIONALISM
Key Data & Facts at a Glance
•

•

•

•

Religious Demographics (Census 2011):
○ Composition: Hindus (79.8%), Muslims (14.2%), Christians (2.3%), Sikhs (1.7%),
Buddhists (0.7%), Jains (0.4%).
○ Linguistic Diversity: India has 22 Scheduled Languages and thousands of dialects, often
overlapping with religious and regional identities, creating a complex social matrix.
Communal and Ethnic Conflict:
○ Communal Riots: India witnessed 59 communal riots in 2024, an 84% increase from 32
incidents in 2023. Religious festivals and processions were the most common triggers for
these riots (CSSS Report, Jan 2025).
○ Fatalities in Riots: A total of 13 lives were lost in the communal riots of 2024, of which
10 were Muslims and 3 were Hindus.
○ Ethnic Conflict (Manipur, 2023-Present): The ongoing conflict between the Meitei and
Kuki-Zo communities has resulted in over 250 deaths and the displacement of more than
60,000 people, highlighting the violent potential of ethnic tensions.
○ Crimes under SC/ST Act: Over 67,000 cases were registered in 2022 for atrocities against
Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes, indicating the persistence of caste-based
violence (NCRB 2022).
Regional Disparity:
○ North-South Economic Divide: Southern states (Karnataka, Tamil Nadu, etc.) contribute
disproportionately more in taxes compared to the fiscal transfers they receive from the
Centre. This creates friction in fiscal federalism.
○ GSDP Performance: States in the Western and Southern regions have consistently
outperformed others. In 2023-24, the per capita income of Goa, Sikkim, and Haryana
was more than 1.7 times the national average, while states like Bihar and Uttar Pradesh
remained significantly below it (EAC-PM Report).
Inter-Community Social Relations:
○ Inter-religious Marriages: Remain very low at around 2.1%. Recent controversies around
anti-conversion laws in several states reflect societal and political resistance to such
unions.
○ Inter-caste Marriages: While still low, they are more common than inter-religious
unions, at 5.8% (NFHS-5, 2021).

Glossary of Essential Concepts
•

•

•
•

Secularism (Indian Context): Unlike the Western model, it is not a strict "wall of separation"
but a doctrine of "Principled Distance" (a term by scholar Rajeev Bhargava). The state treats
all religions with equal respect (Sarva Dharma Samabhava) but can intervene in religious
affairs to uphold constitutional values like equality and social justice.
Secularization vs. Secularism: Secularism is a political doctrine or state policy. Secularization
is a societal process involving the gradual decline of religious influence in public life. A key
paradox in India is having a secular state within a deeply religious, and not fully secularized,
society.
Communalism: An ideology that weaponizes religious identity for political ends. It posits that
people of the same religion have common, and often antagonistic, secular (political,
economic) interests compared to other religious groups.
Regionalism: An ideology and political movement seeking to advance the interests of a
specific geographical region over national interests, often based on a shared culture,
language, or sense of neglect.

AHMEDABAD | BENGALURU | BHOPAL | CHANDIGARH | DELHI | GUWAHATI | HYDERABAD | JAIPUR | JODHPUR | LUCKNOW | PRAYAGRAJ | PUNE | RANCHI
